A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the bolder of policy No. 278,127 a cheque for
£1,099 13s. 6d. in payment of the cash va'ue of the Bonus for 1891, the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these i o icies are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured.

of which upwards of £17,400,000 were bonus payments —more than twice the amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company.

The Co-Operative
SYSTEM, the nearest approach to Socialism that can be achieved under the
present conditions of life, has met with phenomenal success wherever it has
been applied to any object. In England, even the Co-operative Societies
benefit some millions of persons—their membership exceeding a million and
a-half, and hold invested capital to the amount of eleven millions of money.
This success is owing to the fact that the investor sees not only a fair com-
mercial return on his investment, but a proprietary division of profits, which
he would not receive from any other organisation. It has been left to Aus-
tralia to apply this communistic principle to

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 10, 1891.
"Do the Australians Hate England ?”
The more or less distinguished visitor who
drifts around for a brief period in Australia
and then bolts back to his native land, and
delivers lectures, or publishes his “impres-
sions,” is fast becoming a bye-word and a
gigantic laughing-stock. David Christie
Murray, the last addition to this great tribe,
has just gone the same way as the rest.
There is a sameness, like that of a literary
Sahara, among these travellers, who all
go back to bang discords on the one key-
note. The feeling of solemn disappointment
which pervades them is almost too deep for
words. The burden of their cry is that the
Australian is not English in his thoughts or
aspirations—that he has little respect for
British traditions or institutions— that he
has no pride in British history—that he looks
with cold, dull indifference on British ideals
in general—and that even the inestimable'
boon of Imperial Federation is, in his case,
only a pearl cast before swine. The whole
race of British travellers, right down to the
lamented barn-stormer who so recently
quitted these shores, are surprised and dis-
gusted by these things. They are utterly
unable to realise that ideals won’t bear tran
shipment, and that traditions can’t be for-
warded by cable. They have almost all
made up their minds that the Australian is
some variety of an Englishman, and starting
from this point they naturally conclude that
he is a very bad variety—which is much the
same as complaining of a horse because it is
a great failure when regarded as a cow.
Some day, possibly, a universal law Avill be
passed by which no man shall be permitted
to write a book about any country until after
ten years’ acquaintance with it, but until
some such statute is enacted, the case is evi-
dently hopeless.
# # *
Yet, strange to relate, tne majority of
“ distinguished visitors ” have returned
home to report that Australia is pro-
foundly loyal to Britain and the British
Crown. However, the last addition to
the tribe announces in the Contemporary
Review that Australia loathes Britain and
would gladly kick the British Grown, which
is a refreshing change from the old mono-
tonous war-whoop of the globe-trotters who
have gone before. But regarded as a histori-
cal statement it is almost as hopelessly and
utterly absurd as the other. Australia does
not love or adore or unanimously grovel to
Britain, nor does it hate that far-away island
which lies off the north-west coast of France,
or long to wipe it out, or desire to do any-
thing for it or against it. Its attitude is one
of cold, immoveable, cast-iron indifference.
But the very fact that any English-speaking
nation can be utterly indifferent to the exis-
tence of England is the one point that is
beyond the range of British comprehension.
Adoration and hatred are active national
emotions, and they both imply that this
country takes a deep and lasting interest in
the so-called fatherland, and as the fixed
idea of the Anglican globe - trotter is
that his fatherland is the hub of the
universe and that the whole human race
lias its eye riveted thereon, he 'can
understand either sentiment. But even if
three million tongues yell the fact into the
capacious ear of Britain that this Southern
race has no sentiments at all on the subject,
it would probably make the matter no
clearer. The English nation itself has no
animosity towards the North Pole or the
bottom of the Pacific ; it certainly doesn’t
go around cheering for them, but, despite
that fact, it has no active dislike for either
of them; and the feeling of Australia towards
its alleged parent is exactly the same. The
one fact which the ‘’distinguished visitor”
always forgets to gum into his hat, is that
Australia is just about as enthusiastic con-
cerning England as it is concerning the east
coast of Mexico, and for want of this text to
start from he inevitably goes astray.
* * #
It is part of the indictment levelled against
this new race that it has no ideals or historical
sympathies ; that it doesn’t thrill” at such
great names as those of Simon de Montfort
and a hundred other mighty patriots, or at
such great memories as those of Cressy,
Poictiers, Trafalgar and Waterloo. And the
statement is true, for the Australian doesn’t
thrill worth a cent. It is all a part of the
same, unspeakable indifference. Thrill is a
thing which can't be transplanted, and time
and distance are fatal to it. The English-
man himself, for that matter, has about
forgotten Simon de Montfort, and not one
in forty knows whether he was a patriot or
a racehorse. And with regard to the old
historic memories, the Briton doesn’t grow
excited to-day over the glorious fight
at Arbela, or the great struggle at Nico-
polis, or that last grand effort that was
made by a dying nation at Karkar,
simply because he was absent ; and he
drops all mention of the great battle of
Baylen out of his history of the Peninsular
war because he wasn’t there ; and the Aus-
tralian in like manner has no interest in
Cressy or Trafalgar because he wasn’t there
either. No nation ever did howl perman-
ently over an event in which it had no share.
The Australian when he grows a national
hero has as much capacity for frenzy as can
be expected. He has done his uttermost to
make a martial demi-god out of stout old
Peter Lalor ; he still goes looking at
intervals for Leichhardt who was lost in the
days of ancient history, and in his anxiety
to find a hero he will probably keep on look-
ing for him for another century or so ; and
he has put a halo round that unspeakable
blunderer, Robert O’Hara Burke. He has
spent so much devotion over these fifth-rate
deities that when he gets a real, first-class
divinity of his own his whoop will assuredly
shake the earth. But he doesn’t want to
borrow a hero, any more than the English-
man wants to borrow Confucius and Gari-
baldi, and keep holiday in honour of them.
* * *
When Imperial Federation fell dead some
few years ago the itinerant Briton struggled
hard to find out the reason why, that he
might go home and incorporate the fact in
his lectures or his big book. So far as Aus-
tralia was concerned he is still investigating
and getting no nearer a solution. Yet the
explanation was sufficiently obvious : Federa-
tion died from utter indifference. There was
no profit in it; so far as this continent was
concerned, in fact, it was plainly a distinct
loss. But the typical Englishman believed
—and still believes-—that Australia and the
other outlying states of the empire must
crave for a share and a place in the British
Parliament, and he was utterly unable to
realise that the British Parliament was of no
more interest to anyone outside of Britain
than the Servian Skuptschina. He believed
that the people of the Antipodes must long
to have a vested interest in the memories cf
Henry Y., of Drake, Wellington, Nelson,
Marlborough, and a hundred similar indi-
viduals, just as the inflated Copt may possibly
imagine that England is longing to federate
with him so as to be able to brag about the
Pharaohs, He had a certain article to sell
—namely, a political alliance, and he thought
fit to decorate and put frills upon the deal
by lectures on the doings of his dead and
buried ancestors. The proceeding was as
dismal in its ghastly absurdity as if, while
trading off a pound of tea, he had delivered
an impassioned address on the Battle of
Waterloo ; and he might as reasonably have
quoted Waterloo as a reason why,anybody
should buy the tea when he didn’t use that
article. Australia declined Federation sim-
ply because it didn’t use it, and the British
patriot still stands aghast with his unfinished
historical lecture in his hand, and tries un-
successfully to realise the meaning of the
situation. /
* * #
Yet Australia, as we have already pointed
out, does not hate Britain, as Mr. Christie
Murray so rashly assumes. Neither does it
collectively hate the British monarchy or the
British monarch. It regards the latter just
as the monarch herself regards Thomas
Jones, of Thursday Island; she doesn’t
cheer wildly for Jones, nor does she regard
him with an active and well-defined loathing,
fche simply doesn’t care one solitary straw
whether there is such a person or not so
long as he abstains from interfering with
her comfort, and the sentiment is mutual.
When the British illustrated paper or the
British cablegram fiend or the British lec-
turer or poet talks incessantly about
Victoria, he excites the same feeling of
temporary disgust that arises in the human
soul when the amateur reciter rises to
deliver, for the thousandth time, the dreary
story of the dreary infant who stood upon a
burning deck, and for the moment the auditor
would gladly kill Victoria and extirpate the
partially combusted infant. When neither
of them are obtrusively put forward he
utterly forgets their existence. The Aus-
tralian is a Republ'can as a matter of busi-
ness. He does not hate England any more
than he hates Panama or Timbuctoo, and
his fixed resolve to be free from outside
interference, and to have no share in
extraneous wars and extraneous politics, is a
sentiment that has no more connection with
England—so long as that country does not
resort to hostile proceedings—than it has
with Central Africa. If a respectable Briton
such as David Christie Murray declines
to share a bed in a hotel with a gentlemanly
Asiatic, it does not necessarily imply that he
abhors the Asiatic in question and is thirst-
ing for his blood ; it merely indicates that
he wants a bed of his own. And the two
cases are absolutely identical. Australia
does not desire to have any political connec-
tion with Britain for exactly the same reason
why Britain does not desire to have any
political connection with Peru. There is
nothing to be gained by it, and even if the
Peruvian threw in gratis the glorious
memories of Pizarro and Almagro it
would probably make no difference. It
makes no difference to us either.
* # *
Even the hazy dream of a great Federa-
tion of all the English-speaking races can
arouse no enthusiasm in this new continent,
and the Englishman wonders much thereat.
But then the latter individual’s enthusiasm
arises solely from his inability to realise the
situation. If the English-speakipg races are
ever united, the centre of power must go
along with the centre of population, and
then the United States will have the control-
ling influence in the new Commonwealth,
and Governor Hamilton K. Fiskb, of
Massachusetts, will be sent over to look after
the province of England, and to assent to
Bills and wear a cocked hat in London.
Britain drivels at intervals about this great
alleged Federation simply because it can’t
understand how, under any possible com-
bination of circumstances, it. can be anything
save the boss and supreme head of the
Anglo-Saxon League. When the fact pene-
trates its pachydermatous intelligence that
such a combination will reduce it to the rank
of a subordinate province it will drop the sub-
ject, for it doesn’t want Governor Hamilton
K. Fiske on any terms. He might be the
best and noblest of mankind, but England
would refuse a foreign apostle on such condi-
tions. Australia feels on the subject of
Fiske just as England does, and Kintore
or Jersey are only Fiske under another
designation—therefore it doesn’t want to
join any outside Federation whatsoever.
This is the sentiment which the British
literary globe-trotter has tried so often to
understand, and always without success,
because he never can and never will judge
any other nation by the same standard as he
judges himself. Possibly if he would picture
his own feelings on the arrival of the first
American Governor of England he might get
some glimmering of the real nature of the
situation.
# » #
Christie Murray assumes that the objec-
tions raised by Australia to promiscuous and
wholesale British immigration arise from the
Australian’s permanent and deep-rooted
hatred to England and all its works. The
conclusion is an idiotic one, and it is idioti-
cally arrived at. It might as reasonably be
assumed that if the litterateur himself objects
to a cow in his bedroom, therefore he loathes
the whole race of cows, calves, bulls, and
heifers, and hungers to reduce them all to
one ruined mass of horns and hoofs and hair
and dead meat. Australia objects to the
new and fresh Englishman chiefly because he
wants to run this country on English plans
and specifications, and for the sole honour
and glory of England. The political situa-
tion would be exactly the same if about
20,000,000 Frenchmen settled in England
and wanted to run England purely for the
glory and profit of France. Even if there
was abundant elbow-room for them all, the
patriotic Briton would doubtless object voci-
ferously ; but, unluckily, he seems utterly
unable to comprehend why anybody should
object, save himself. He argues exclusively
upon the theory that because there is an
abundance of empty space in Australia there-
fore the English immigrant must be wanted to
fill that space. But as it happens space and
emptiness are merely relative terms Aus-
tralia is empty comnared with England ;
England is also empty c< mpared with the
provinces of eastern China ; and the reason
why Australia should welcome the British
immigrant irrespective of numbers is exactly
the same reason why Britain should take in
some 20,000,000 Chinamen, and feel glad to
see them come. Yet if the Briton will look
at the matter carefully he will find that he
isn’t pining for 20,000,000 or any less
number of Chinamen, despite the com-
parative emptiness of his country, and when
he has fully analysed this emotion he may
possibly understand why the Australian
doesn’t pine after the imported English-
man, despite the emptiness - likewise
comparative —of his country ul.o The
_ -»
position in both cases is sufficiently simple
and if the globe-trotter understands the one
problem, yet can’t realise the other, it can
only be because his Creator didn’t furnish
him with comprehension, and no one else
can supply the deficiency. So far, he has
shown no signs of understanding iu the
matter When the hospitable Australian
took him in and entertained him, he set it
down to .profound and undying loyalty to
England, just as Cetbwayo, when he was
made a temporary lion in London, might
have set that down to England’s love and
abject reverence for his far-off Zululand.
And when, as iu the case of Christie
Murray, this country guyed his execrable
show, that was set down to a national hatred
that nothing could ever quench or alleviate.
And yet in reality there is neither love nor
hatred in the case —only profound indiffer-
ence.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Blessing's in Disguise.
A writer in London Referee repeats the
Bulletin’s old argument that an exceptionally
insane or blackguardly peer is a noble blessing
in disguise, and says of the impecunious young
Ailesbury :—
, I[e is probably doing more service to his country than
nine-tenths of his fellow-peers. Who knows what national
benefit may not come of his peculiar reading of the
proverb Noblesse oblige ? He is bringing into disrepute
{more disrepute—En. B.) the principle of hereditary
nobility and primogeniture, two of our national institu-
tions which, dating from the dark ages, seem destined
sooner or later, to be swept away in the tide of national
progress. . . . The evils of privilege are considerably
modified by our system of constantly refreshing the
Upper Chamber by infusions of plebeian blood (mostly
brewers, by the way), and, failing the revelation of some
glaring wrong, the English people are hard to move in
the direction of reform. But it does seem odd that, while
the Jockey Club may purify Newmarket of the Marquis
of Ailksbury’s presence, no means should exist whereby
this hereditary legislator can be warned off the benches
of the House of Lords.
The Referee man doesn’t stick to his text,
though. Theoretically, the foul-mouthed hus-
band of Dolly Tester is as well entitled to sit
on those benches as his respectable brother-
Marquis of Salisbury, the type of man who so
“ fills the position ” he was born into as to
almost hide the fact that said position is
founded on hereditary wrong and covered with
a moss of abuses. Noblesse oblige is, indeed, the
falsest of proverbs, implying that ruling fami-
lies are estimable institutions as long as they
are represented by decent individuals. Yet a
bad old Tory principle is equally bad whether
illustrated by a sober Cecil or a boozy Brude-
nell-Bruce. The one treats his subject in a
glamourous, dignified style, the other gives us
a hideous caricature, and it is the caricature
which disgusts the vulgar eye and sets the
masses thinking. People become suddenly
aware that the marquis doesn’t make the man.
A little mental effort lands them at the con-
clusion that the man doesn’t make the marquis.
He is a creature of scandalous circumstances,
for which he is no more to blame than a rabbit
is to blame for being a devouring calamity. If
any marquis desired to make over his estates
tor the benefit of mankind the law of the land
would step in and stop him with an in-
junction, even as it has recently pro-
hibited Ailesbury from yielding up his
countless acres of Savernake to Sam Lewis, the
money lender. What has Ailesbury’s indi-
vidual folly and profligacy to do with his claim
to sit in the House of Peers, where a seat was
guaranteed to the young idiot by an idiotic
constitution before he was born ? Nothing. The
British nation took its chance of Ailesbury,
just as it took its chance of Tummy, whose
“right” to the Throne is not a question of
whether he carries baccarat-counters or a Bible
in his coat-pocket. Republicanism doesn’t
object to the Monarchy or the House of Peers
on purely personal grounds, although it is neces-
sary to make the best use of personal objects of
ridicule against those institutions and their
like. The real grievance is that toilers are
taxed, directly and indirectly, anil manhood is
in a hundred ways degraded in order that social
and political supremacy may bd assured to
two classes of money-spenders, whose education
almost invariably warps their virtues, when
they have any to start with, and quite invari-
ably brings their worst vices to a full maturity.
Harper on Intelligence.
In the Victorian Assembly the other night
Harper, M.L.A., the illustrious pepper-
grinder, rose like a thick stone wall to
block Premier Munro’s “One Man One
Vote ” Bill, and rashly stated his reasons for
so doing. When the modern Conservative gives
his reasons he is generally a lost bigot, and
Harper went the way of all the rest—yet his
case was the best one that was advanced on the
Tory side all through the debate. ■“ Men should
not have equal powers at elections,” he said,
“because some are much more capable than
others.” The Bulletin cordially concurs
in this statement, but has to remark
that Harper left the observation unfinished,
and an unfinished observation is of little service
to anybody. The Bulletin, like Harper,
would willingly give two, or even twelve, votes
to the man with brains if it could define that
man, and only one vote or no vote at all
to the man without brains, if it could define
that man also ; but as both men are generally
undefinable, the scheme is impossible. And,
strangely enough, Harper offered no sug-
gestion as to how the brainy man was to
be discovered and to get his extra votes.
His own idea evidently was that he should go
round in person, and differentiate the man of
large capacity from the other man with no
capacity at all, but in that case he would
assuredly put himself at the head of the list,
which would be sufficient evidence that the
examiner of intelligence had none himself.
The simplest wav, no doubt, would be for
each voter to slate candidly whether he
was supplied with intellect or not, but
then, who could swear that the returns
were reliable, and where is the voter who would
say he hadn’t an intellect ? Another method


would be for each man to decide on the brains
of the man next door, in which case Harper
would doubtless report that the common fellow
who repaired the road in front of his mansion
had only enough capacity for one vote, and
that son of toil would quite as probably return
a verdict that Harper himself was an unspeak-
able ass who couldn’t be trusted with any vote
at all. If Ibis system would be satisfactory to
Harper he ought to have mentioned the fact,
rphere would be nothing to be gained by
measuring the head of each voter in order to
arrive at an idea of the size of his thinking
organ, for the man with the small head might
have brain inside, and the man with the large
one might have only water and a tumour ; and
it is impossible to judge anybody’s mental
capacity by his property, for he might have
got it in the same way as he got his large feet
and his short legs —by inheritance, in which case
he might as well get an extra vote for
the short legs as for the property. Moreover,
he might have stolen the property, and in that
case he would have an extra vote for his
dishonesty, which is a depressing considera-
tion, and one which might lead to garrotting
and pocket-picking. Therefore, to all appear-
ance, the project is unworkable. If it could be
managed The Bulletin would be glad, for
then George Higinbotham would have at
least 48 times as many votes as Harper, which
would be a fair and reasonable average ; but
unhappily there is no way of arriving at it, and
the only alternative is to give every man one
vote, and thus strike the best average that the
case will admit. It is better that a great and
comprehensive fool should have one vote than
twenty, anyhow.
♦
In Bathurst (N.S.W.) the price of gas—manu-
factured in the corporation gas-works —has
just been reduced to 3s. per 1000 feet. In
Sydney, where the gas-supply is in the hands
of a big private company, the price is ss. 3d.
Yet the gas-accounts at Bathurst show a profit,
from which, we presume, that the Sydney
monopoly could supply gas at 3s. 6d. or so, if it
felt that way inclined, and show a profit also.
But it is the chief disadvantage of the average
monopoly that it never does feel that way
inclined, and it is more likely to raise the price
of its faded yellow glimmer than to reduce it.
Therefore, when gas is a dead and gone illumi-
nation, and electricity has taken its place, the
people of Sydney (who are just now being
asked to sanction a private Electric Light-
ing Bill) should make sure that the light-
ing business is retained under municipal
control then and for ever afterwards. Mel-
bourne suffers even more seriously from its gas-
syndicate, but the soulful corporation in the
Victorian capital seems to regret that it is a
monopoly, and it wants badly to sell out to the
ratepayers, and points out incessantly that the
city ought to retain the profits of the in-
dustry in its own hands. It has felt this way
ever since the electric-light began to run it out
of the field a year or two ago, and in the full-
ness of its heart it has done its utmost ever
since then to trade off its goodwill and its
ancient plant for about three times what they
were worth when new. The deal has nearly
come off more than once, but every time some-
thing prevented it, and the company’s offer has
thus been a blocked generosity. It feels badly
hurt over this, too ; there is something almost
tearful in the way it implores the city‘to come
in and share the profits, and even when the
city rejects the offer in a brutal, unfeeling
manner, it always comes back and starts the
subject over again at next municipal meeting.

Sydney D.T. is hysterical on the subject of
“Victoria, the Free Pass Happy Land.” But is
Hot New South Wales infected body and soul
with the “ deadhead ” disease? In Victoria the
evil is exposed by a Parliamentary committee,
but in New South Wales a demand for an inquiry
is bumptiously converted into a motion of cen-
sure. The “free-pass” section of the com-
munity would constitute a little army in New
South Wales. Not content with their £3OO a
year, and free carriage in the State trains,
members of Parliament ask for and receive free
passes for their wives and families and servants.
Even Ministers of the Crown arc commonly
known to take advantage of their Ministerial
positions to give a free ride to a relative or in-
fluential constituent. No traveller can be blind
to the rascally doings of certain politicians.
The usual practice is for a Minister or a
meraber-out-of-office to “engage” a car, and
take in his wife and children, with perhaps a
few friends. A Minister has been known to
put off a journey because the necessary “free
passes ” had not been forwarded for his wife,
family and servants. The “ dead-head ” system
must cost an enormous sum every year. But
no reform will ever take place while a majority
of the Assembly grovel to the Commissioners.
It pays a married politician to uphold the
“ free-pass ” institution. We have just heard of
a rich New South Wales official who, for purely
ornamental duties, is paid £BOO a year, and
whose place could be easily supplied at less
than £3OO a year—yet he is given the use of
a Government steam-launch and crew to convey
his female relations and their luggage to 1m sea-
side residence ! Free passes indeed !
A country which allows lawyers to make it®
laws is a fool of a country. In Australia the
law is mostly made by lawyers, and con-
sequently it is hardly possible to understand
any Act without hiring a solicitor to explain it,
or to do anything under the provisions of any
Act without hiring a lawyer, and getting
counsel’s opinion. Every year, too, a dozen
new laws are passed, requiring the nation to
1 .re still more lawyers, and it is hardly possible
to do anything without filing a document, or
swearing an affidavit, or lodging a caveat,
or a demurrer, or something, at great
expense. In Norway every town or village has
its board of conciliation elected by the in-
habitants. Everybody who wants to go to law
has to bring his case before this board, and the
plaintiff and the defendant are both required to
dump down the sum of for expenses. The
board isn’t absolutely ravenous about the
however; it gets it whenever it can, but if
either party hasn’t the money it is bound to go
°n with the case just the same. No lawyers
are allowed to appear before the court unless
one of the parties to the case is a foreigner
residing outside the country—an event which
hardly ever happens; the plaintiff or defendant
must turn up in person, or hy deputy, and the
deputy may he any sort of individual under the
sun, except a lawyer. If the defendant refuses
to appear, or if either party refuses to accept
the decision, then the case goes to law; the
board of conciliation has no power to enforce its
decision beyond this—the individual who de-
clines to submit his case to it, or to accept its
award, has to pay all the costs of the subsequent
proceedings whether he wins or loses. When
the board settles a case it charges the losing
party a further sum of So that the entire
cost of the biggest lawsuit is 2s. 7|d. The
boards decide 88 per cent, of the Norwegian
litigation, and the lawyers get the remaining
12 per cent. only. In almost all business con-
tracts a clause is now inserted that in case of
any dispute the decision of the board shall be
final, so that the percentage of actual litigation
is still diminishing. The courts sit once a week
in towns, and once every fortnight or every
month in country districts ; and as no case can
be adjourned more than once, nobody ever waits
more than two months at the outside for a
decision. This is the sort of law which
Australia wants badly.
The Victorian Upper House tackled the
Federation Bill, the other night, and abolished
that obnoxious and revolutionary term, the
“Commonwealth” (which is Saxon), on the
ground that it wasn’t good “English.” Then
it inserted the term “Dominion” (which is
Latin) on the ground that it teas good English !
and went on its way rejoicing. Probably the
Upper House hadn’t a dictionary Within reach,
or else it had one and looked for “ Common-
wealth ” under “ K. ” Both the terms are now
passably good English, but the former has
the additional advantage of being good Austra-
lian as well. But “Commonwealth” signifies
in the largest sense, government by the people ;
it has a smothered aspect of socialism, republi-
canism, and land nationalisation about it; while
“Dominion” is a good word to grovel about,
for it seems to imply that the country is
the personal property of a monarch, and the
average Legislative councillor loves to talk in
unctuous tones about his beloved sovereign and
her dominions. It is impossible to yell for a
beloved monarch and her commonwealth ; and
so the fatted orator at a banquet would be left.
He has a very limited repertoire, has that
maudlin fat party who rises and totters on his
fiat feet at the Queen’s Birthday feed, and if
the “dominions” were struck out he would
have no repertoire at all. No one can imagine
a huge red person hollering for a commonwealth
in any case ; somehow, it is always the thin
man with only one chin who wants a common-
wealth, and the vast individual with grease
and wine on his whiskers wants a dominion.
Nobody knows exactly why this is, but the
fact is indisputable. If you weigh twenty
typical loyalists and twenty average repub-
licans, there is at least one and a-half tons
difference in the total—sometimes more.
The organ of which Henry George lately
resigned the editorship demands to know (in
the interests of Freetrade and unlimited Free
Competition) why America does not take the
Russian Hebrew in—5,000,000 of him—and
treat him as a brother? The two following
pictures of human inhumanity are the gist of
the argument: —
A number of Hebrew immigrants from Russia arriving
in the ports of New York and Boston have been refused
permission to land because of their poverty. Was there
ever a more cruel exhibition of “ man’s inhumanity to
man ?” The whole civilised world is shocked and horrified
by the brutal banishment of these people from their
homes by Russia’s ruthless and abhorrent Czar. Nowhere
is this feeling more general than in the United States ;
but when a few of the persecuted wanderers reach our
shores we practically say to them : “ Go back to death at
the hands of the tyrant or fling yourselves into the sea,
for here, in ‘ the home of the brave and the land of the
free,’ there is no room for such as you.”
Years ago the writer saw a little boy, whose hands had
been mangled by a railway-train, run through the streets
with fingers hanging by a shred from his bleeding hands.
He undertook to enter a humble home, perhaps his own,
when a stalwart woman literally slammed the door in his
face, knocking him down, possibly to save her carpets
The writer had forgotten the incident until this parallel
act of cruelty by a great nation recalled it to his mind.
Exactly so. And now we want to know if
George’s literary successor took that boy in
and let him bleed over his carpets, or did be
leave bim out on the pavement, and go away to
make a paragraph out of the incident, and sell
the par. for cash to a newspaper. Also,
we would like to know if he has taken in even
one foul, evil-smelling, tattered Russian Hebrew
and let him roost in the Standard office, or is
he only cursing other people, in both cases, for
leaving undone what lie hasn’t done himself.
Sjuch philanthropy and such freetrade are both
exceedingly cheap. If the United States takes
in the whole gang of battered, penniless Israel-
ites there isn’t one of them who will run a news-
paper—in the English language at all events—
and thus damage George’s business, and he
nobly doesn’t care a hang though they damage
everybody else’s business. He wants the whole
5,000,000 to walk right in and make a mess on
somebody else’s carpet. And that is the I rec-
trader all over.

Sydney D.T. complains
The gratitude of Turkey is not retrospective in its view.
It lies mainly in the direction of favours to come. It is
nothing to her that England has fought her battles and
filled her empty coffers time after time at the cost of
English investors impoverished by her dishonored bonds,
and she yields but languid acquiescence to the fact that
but for the lavish expenditure of British blood and
treasure the Czar would have reigned in Constantinople
generations ago.
The amount of ignorance and astonishing
stupidity that is compressed into that one short
paragraph is amazing. What in the name of
all that is astounding does Turkey owe any
gratitude for ? In the reign of Mahmoud, Eng-
land, France and Russia swooped down on her
in a time of alleged friendship and alliance,
and without even declaring war they destroyed
her fleet at Navarino ; and then England
backed out and left her so crippled by this
disaster that she fell an easy prey to the
Czar. In 1854 England and France stood
by until Omar Pasha had utterly de-
feated the Russians by his own efforts, and
refused to move a gun or a man until the last
Muscovite had been driven headlong out of
Turkey; and then, when the victorious Moslems
had a prospect of peace and tranquillity, they
were forced by their two useless allies to waste
their men and money in the useless invasion or
the Crimea. In the next war bn gland saa cd
Constantinople, and calmly took Cyprus in
payment, and Turkey could have purchased
the support of alftiost any of the Great Powers
at the same price. She simply bought England s
assistance at a most exorbitant rate, because
the Sultan had no time to haggle over terms.
Since then, England has stolen Egypt 111
a time of profound peace, and another great
hunk of the Moslbm states has thus vanished.
In between these events, British financiers
have lent money to the Porte at nearly 40 pci
cent. interest, discount, and other steals
all included—and have thus ruined her over
again, and finally England threatens to grab
Mitylene —also in a time of peace. lurthei,
she engaged to prevent Russia building a Black
Sea fleet and broke her word; she engaged
again to keep thfe Czar’s fleet out of the Medi-
terranean and broke that treaty also, aim she
broke every other treaty she ever made at Con-
stantinople. And, therefore, Turkey longs to
meet England some day or other in perdition,
and there howl curses and heave molten fire at
her for 20,000,000 centuries for her treachery
and lying, and the Moslems are not grateful
not much.

The Canadian Premier has found himself obliged
to bring forward a measure which purports to
deal a death-blow to bribery ; but it may fairly
be maintained that any Bill making penal both
the offering and the acceptance of bribes will
defeat its own ends. The only certain means
of checking bribery would be the introduction
of a measure abolishing all penalties for offering
bribes, while providing for the condign punish-
ment of those accepting the same. It is an open
secret that in one of the Australias, a few years
since, a Minister of the Crown was guilty of
gross corruption in straining his power for the
purpose of blackmailing individuals; and the
evil result of this Minister’s conduct is still
felt in the province, although his portfolio
is now in the hands of a man of unblemished
reputation. Had the means for checking
bribery been in force there can be no doubt that
this blackmailing, which is supposed to have
aggregated at least £30,000, would have been
impossible, in addition to which the public
interests would have been far better served,
and the man on whose recommendation this
undoubtedly astute individual received his ap-
pointment would have been spared considerable
odium. In N. S. Wales the absurdity of the
law as it now stands has been shown by the
action some time back of Premier Parkes in
prosecuting an unfortunate individual who had
the impertinence to offer him a trifling sum with
a view to obtaining a small Government ap-
pointment, while it is certain that no protection
from blackmailing such as mentioned above is
provided. What both Australia and Canada
really require is such an alteration in their
statutes as will make it no longer equally neces-
sary for those offering bribes to keep silent, as
it is for those accepting the same.
Dear Bulletin —Except for the discredit of
the thing—for what professional journalist who
honoured his calling could enter into a competi-
tion on such degrading terms ? I would
contribute a yarn to the “ Christmas Story
Competition ” of a certain paper, on the
off-chance of winning, not the prize, but a
place among the number of rejected stories
worthy of publication. And what would Ido
upon the publication of my yarn ? Eire a bill
into the proprietary for payment at ordinary
rates ! The smart Editor and his smarter eo-
proprietor have been just a little too clever this
time. One of the conditions of the competitions
confers upon the Editor the right to publish
any story not taking the prize. To have
appended to this clause the words “ without
payment ” would have given the game
straight away (it is to be presumed that
the paper is playing the office’s old
game) "and, accordingly, the words were left
out. But the omission, as I am legally
advised, will not remove the obligation to
pay for all competing stories inserted. I
have withheld this bit of information till the
“competition” has virtually closed, so that no
amendment of the condition can be made ; and
I am prepared to back my solicitor s judgment
in the matter to the extent of a two-guinea
subscription to have the point tested in court,
should the paper carry out its editor s implied
threat of publishing a selection from the non-
prize-winning manuscripts. Something will
have to be done to discourage the continuance
in connection witli the Australian press of a
method of procuring original matter which
would bring a blush to the unwashed cheek of a
Seven Dials publisher. Even Jemmy Gatnach
paid for the screed he printed.—A Hireling
Scribe.
While the New South Wales Government
reforming banking legislation it will be well if
it arranges that the banks where Government
loan funds are lodged are on a thoroughly
sound basis. As things at present stand, the
bank auditors never even see the branch
accounts or securities, and even the securities
at the head-office are merely passed as stated,
and no idea of their value is ever attempted to
be obtained by auditors. The issue of notes
uncontrolled by Government is another bad
point, for supposing that the general manager
of a bank having 50 branches wishes to put a
good face on the balance sheet, he can easily
send to each branch 10,000 new, unsigned notes.
These stand in the books of the branch as cash,
but pay no duty and are not advised to head
office as issued until actually signed and paid
out. The funds in hand thus appear to be half
a million in excess of actual fact. The only
absolute remedy is to make the issue of
notes a State monopoly, but till this is
done all notes should be obtained by the
banks through Government, duly stamped with
the amount of duty for one year, which should
be paid by the banks on receipt of the notes,
and these should be liable to duty until with-
drawn from circulation when cancelled by
Government, which could then recover the amount
if any, or make a refund should they be with-
drawn before the expiry of the time on which
duty had been paid. Thus there would be
established a system of control preventing the
issue or use of notes in excess of the amount
legally authorised. And it should be made a
felony for any director or general manager to
authorise or obtain from the bank with which
he is connected any pecuniary advance what-
ever.
Baron Carington will now be able to perceive
what history will think of his connection with
the shameful Mount Rennie affair. Already
there are to be found, in a half-house, 13 mem-
bers of the N.S. Wales Legislative Assembly
who, on account of his share in that dark and
savage deed, resist the naming after him of a
city electoral division; when the generation
which licked his lordly boots shall have passed
away and lie is remembered only as the
man who sent four boys to their death
after a trial which was a farce, his name
will be execrated for all time throughout
the Australian Republic. His measure of igno-
miny is shared not only by all those who had a
direct hand in the unspeakable blasphemy
against justice and civilisation which culminated
on the 4th January, 1887, but also by the daily
press and by every citizen of Australia who,
knowing the revolting circumstances of the so-
called trial, failed to protest against this bloody
perversion of every principle of ‘ ‘ British Jus-
tice.” —
“ Whatever the facilities afforded to prisoners on their
trial may be, they can be of little avail when the sittings
of the Court are protracted from day to day to such an
extreme as to exhaust the physical and mental energies
of counsel and jurymen alike. No case has been heard
of in modern times in which English Judges have com-
pelled jurors and counsel in criminal cases involving
issues of life and death to sit all through the night in
spite of repeated protests and requests for adjourn-
ment.”
So said the cowardly S.M. Herald in a leading
article—but not until the very hour on which
the condemned stood upon the scaffold. “For
such a trial as this,” exclaimed W. B. Halley,
(vide S.M. Herald report of deputation to Gover-
nor Carington) “ one looks in vain even to the
darkest and bloodiest periods of Irish history. '
The Bulletin trusts that the Mount Rennie
affair will again come before Parliament in the
form of a motion for the release of the sur-
viving convicts. Law may keep them in gaol.
Justice cannot do so. They have never yet been
tried.
One Man, One Vote.
“The D.” writes us thus; —Dear Bulletin,—
The One-man-one-vote problem appears to be too
steep an equation for N.S.W. parliamentarians*
Simple and finite as are its terms, members will
persist in regarding it as of the indeterminate
order, only to be solved by the application of the
process of “continued fractions.” This confusion
apparently arises from the ever-changing condi-
tions of population in the various areas of a young
and progressive nation. One nostrum is the
division of the province into electorates, each
returning a single member. Tins would be seem-
ingly fair if district populations always remained
stationary. Notoriously, they do not, however,
and this circumstance would necessitate a redis-
tribution of electorates at least every three years,
if not oftener. The parliamentary manipulation
of such a periodical task would mean artful divi-
sion of the most party kind, and under which the
uppermost dog would maintain and increase pre-
sent advantages. An escape from such a tyranny
has been suggested by the appointment of a non-
political Board which shall, without reference to
Parliament or the Ministry, periodically re-divide
the country into the required number of elec-
torates. This sounds plausible enough, but when-
ever was there a Ministerially-appointed Board
that had no political bias, or that was not squeez-
able? Never. Another hon. member has—or,
rather, several hon. members have—been illogical
enough to contend that country districts require
a greater proportion of representation than do
metropolitan electorates. This proposal would
simply split into posts-and-rails the solid log of
One-man-one-vote, and it may be contemptuously
dismissed. The one scheme that presents a per-
fect theory of equal representation on the basis of
One-man-one-vote —or, as it might then be styled,
Each-man-equal-voting-power—is to convert New
South Wales into one huge single electorate, and
place within the power of every elector the right
to vote for as many candidates as there are
representatives in Parliament. The politicians,
of course, will howl down any such proposal.
Candidates would then have practically to canvass
the whole colony—if not by actually stumping it,
at least, by plenitude of advertisements and
by filling the land with printed addresses.
Minister Garruthebs, for instance, already snorts
because the area of the Sturt electorate covers
45,000 square miles, and gives a daisy job to can-
vassers. But which are the interests to be mainly
considered in an electioneering maelstrom—those
of the candidates, or those of the electors?
Undoubtedly, the latter. The Legislative
Assembly lias already lightened the woes of can-
didates by abolishing the £4O deposit hitherto
required to be put up as a preliminary guarantee
against a frivolous and unwarranted contest, and
it° might not be a hurtful precaution to hedge
against prolific would-be’s by a counter call of the
kind indicated; although it is possible, if not
probable, that the Legislative Council will
endeavour to re-impose the deposit risk. If, how-
ever, it is not considered desirable to load candi-
dates with adverse contingencies, but it is wished
to give every self-inspired aspirant a full and easy
chance of getting into the legislature, might not
the democracy go further still than the Assembly
has yet essayed ? Might not the whole cost of all
elections be saddled on the State, within
well-defined limits of allowable expenditure?
Might not the Government Gazette be
the medium through which the election addresses
of the several hundreds of candidates shall be
distributed among the electors? For, with no
deposit required, and no election expenses to pro-
vide for, there would probably be ten times as
many candidates as possible members ! Forsaking
the sarcastic mood of the last two sentences, it
may be reiterated that the One-electorate system
is the only perfect theory under which absolute
equality of manhood suffrage can ever be obtained
—a theory, too, which, if memory serves aright,
The Bulletin has favorably recommended in the
past. Whether the adoption of this system would
or would not lead to an organisation of prelimi-
nary caucus-pollings, and whether such caucus-
work would be healthy or otherwise for the body
politic, open up too many considerations for
adequate treatment here.

General Manages
“Independence: A Retrospect.”*
The above is the style and designation of a
pamphlet lately published in London by one
Charles Bland, and purporting to be a
history of the sudden rise and the great
appalling fall of the Australian Republic. The
writer, like Bellamy, is looking backward—
looking with sad regret down the long avenue
of years where lie the ashes and the time-
blasted wreckage of a nation that began its
career without brains or patriotism, and ended
it after the manner of a broken-down shearers’
strike. The writer’s diction is woefully loose,
and his story is feebly strung together, but the
great axiom of his faith is at least unmistake-
able, and that axiom is that Australia is utterly
dependent on England for money, brains, and
political morality, and, therefore, with the
severing of the “silken bonds,” the new
republic slides down with a yell and a curse
into the abyss. From first to last it makes no
attempt to struggle against fate, and the one
bright spot in the story is where the miserable,
toothless commonwealth goes back—crippled,
and in a wheelbarrow—to its allegiance, and
howls outside the door of its political parent
asking to be taken in. It is taken in accordingly
—and the proceedings end with repentance,
tears, and “ God .Save the Queen.” The picture
of the prodigal commonwealth moaning at its
mother’s keyhole amid the frozen blast of a cold
winter of discontent is touchingly drawn.
# # •
Mr. Bland is not, in any sense, original, save
in his unfathomable dullness. His book is
heavier than a lead-mine filled with water,
duller than a toothless aboriginal, and less in-
telligent than a pig’s infant. Even his nomen-
clature is of the bald and hairless variety. The
people of ‘ ‘ Albert-land ” —wherever that may
be—start the great movement by refusing to
receive Sir Henry Thorndyke as- Governor—
an incident which is obviously a slavish copy of
that other famous trouble when Sir Thomas
MTl wraith put his veto upon Policeman
Blake, and abolished that incipient official in
one act. They do this for no possible reason
save larrikinism and cussedness, for all their
relations are peaceful, and the new Governor is
a noble individual—so noble that even to carry
his washing would be in itself a liberal educa-
tion. Then the Australian republic starts
business in such a way as no republic ever
started before. It has no central authority—
no capital—no Federal Parliament—and the six
individual States positively refuse to agree
about anything. Even Liberia, Peru, and the
negro commonwealth of San Domingo knew
better than this, but Australia doesn’t know
better, because the instant it loses the assist-
ance and protection of England it ceases to
know anything. How any other State, which
hasn’t the assistance of England, knows any-
thing either, isn’t explained ; in fact this parti-
cular author explains nothing at all. He men-
tions, however, that after the separation was
accomplished Greek was very little known in
Australia, and that there was no longer at
Government House any “ Lady Violet or Lady
Gwendolen for our girls to imitate in voice
and manner,” and this circumstance seems to
lie heavily on his chest. How nine-tenths of the
world gets on without any Lady Violet, or how
no country, save England, can grow the afore-
said Gwendolen, or why the local girl shouldn’t
struggle through with her own voice, are three
more of the unanswered problems of the story.
# # *
Then the new republic cruises aimlessly
around for a year or two on a sunless sea of
drivel and incapacity. Every other new state
lias grown some great man at the crisis when
its career was just beginning -even San Domingo
produced the mighty Toussatnt L’Ouverture,
and the worn and feeble nations of South
America raised a dozen doughty men, from
Bolivar to Balmaceda ; but the Australian
Commonwealth doesn't grow so much as a
decent parish beadle. When the silken bonds
are severed, the united intellect of the country
turns to road-metal. The whole nation goes
bankrupt almost on the instant, to start with,
because the supply of English loans ran out,
from which fact a great moral is to be deduced,
and that moral is that no nation can exist if
England doesn’t lend it money. How any part
of the world existed at all until some *2OO
years ago, when national debts were first
invented, or how it is that England exists,
seeing that she isn’t borrowing money,
arc two more problems which remain unan-
swered. Anyhow, Australia bursts because it
can't get into debt, and at this crisis Ecuador
rises and commits a brutal outrage on this
country, and this country can do nothing but
submit. It lias to knock under abjectly be-
cause it is no longer joined on to England, and
yet Ecuador isn’t joined on to England either,
and it doesn’t knock under, which is inexplic-
able. Why Ecuador doesn’t go and outrage all
t he other countries that don’t belong to Britain
is another unsettled consideration, and the
question why the North Pole doesn’t commit a
brutal wrong against the bottom of the Atlantic
comes into the same category. As Ecuador
just now has only an army of 1600 men, and
lias no navy at all, and as it is dead-broke, and
t he entire white population is only 100,000, in-
cluding infants and people of deficient intellect,
the natural proceeding would be to crowd the
police force and the Permanent Artillery into
t he Rotomahana, and knock the republic into
slivers, but this -would spoil the story. A
hideous outrage perpetrated by Goat Island
upon the rest of this mighty continent would
have filled the bill just as well.
< * # *
Finally, war breaks out between Britain and
France, and the Australian republic dies miser-
ably. The French cruisers capture the British
mail-boats, &c., on the coast, and this finishes
the abject and imaginary commonwealth. Of
course they would have captured these vessels
just the same if Australia had still belonged to
the Empire, and would probably have burnt
Melbourne and Sydney as well, but the writer
forgets this. In his plain and simple pig-headed-
netis even such an obvious fact as that never
dawns on him. Australia suffers no damage in
the war, but it goes bung because it can’t get
its wool exported, or its gold, or its corn, or
anything else ; ami therefore men give up
raising wool, or mining for gold, or growing
vegetables, and relapse into a state of nature.
In real life, of course, trade would go on just as
before, and American, German, Spanish,
Italian, and other vessels would take up the
business, but the writer of “Independence”
evidently doesn’t know that any country has
ships save England, Also, if Australia—a
purely neutral state—is thus ruined by a war
between two outside parties, it naturally
follows that every other neutral state must be
affected the same way, and all the world must
go to utter, unspeakable smash from exactly
the same causes, but the incredible mule
who perpetrates the book never notices this
tremendous discrepancy. It is at this stage of
the proceedings that the Commonwealth gees
round miserably on all-fours to the back-door
and implores its once-despised mother to take
it in and hide it in a safe place under the wash-
board, and it is taken in accordingly. Presum-
ably, under these circumstances, France goes
on seizing the mail-steamers as before, and,
Australia being now an enemy’s-country, bom-
bards Melbourne, Sydney, Brisbane, and
Adelaide as well—at least, that is what would
assuredly happen in real history—but this
obvious circumstance is judiciously shirked.
The story ends with the landing of the
new Governor—in the depth of its repentance
Australia implores the people at “Home” to
send out the same individual whom it formerly
rejected—and “God Save the Queen” generally,
and the whole nation is wallowing on ail fours.
* * *
“ Independence ” is, in its way, the most hope-
lessly dull, inconsequent book that ever
appeared in print. The writer is a tenth-rate
copyist—a dreary, childish groveller without
sense, logic, or knowledge of mankind. His
republic falls owing to a series of causes which
would make any real and genuine State rise and
prosper ; in fact, he doesn’t know a cause when
he sees it, and having resolved to make the
Commonwealth die he doesn’t know how to kill
it. His diction is as solemn as the ashes of a
dead world ; he hauls his tale in clumsily by
the hair, and hauls it out again in the last
chapter the same way ; he is pompous, dicta-
torial, and hopelessly uninteresting, and he has
a dreary way of holding up his dead facts for
inspection on the end of his literary pitchfork
which is unspeakably irritating. But still his
book has one great recommendation ; as a handy
publication to buy and give to an enemy, it is
unrivalled. Unnecessary to state, it has been
largely eulogised by the Australian daily press,
the owners and conductors whereof were mostly
suckled on such unspeakable bosh in Hingland.
*“ Independence ; a Retrospect,” by Charles Ash-
wold Bland; published by Harrison and Sons, London,
More “National Association.”
Within the last few weeks N.S. Wales has started
a “National Association,” and in proof of its
strictly national character a large gang of in-
fluential capitalists have already joined, and more
are joining. Nobody, in fact, who is not a mer-
chant, or a banker, or a big landholder, or the
owner of a large amount of cash carefully in-
vested, has anything to do with the association.
There is nobody in it who wants the abolition of
plural voting, for the members all have plural
votes —whole stacks of them. There is nobody
who wants land-nationalisation, for they all own
land or have mortgages over it. The National
Association doesn’t personally care about free
education, for it is in a position to pay for
all the education it wants; nor does it
care about a system of universal State
insurance, because it has no immediate [ rospect
of being hard-up in its old age. It is for
these reasons that it is called a “National ” Asso-
ciation. The associates are mostly Foreign-
traders and believers in cheap Chinese, nigger,
and European labour, for all these tilings bring
down wages, and most of them pay wages instead
of receiving them ; and they are decidedly
“loyal,” because some of them have the run of
Government House, and the rest want to have it.
Their programme is borrowed verbatim from that
of the Victorian “National” Association, and
consists of eight articles, of which 50 per cent, are
hypocrisy, and the other 50 are liogwash. The
first of these eight purposes is “ to preserve the
national character of the Legislature, ” and to pre-
vent members being the “delegates of any section
of society,” which obviously means that the mem-
bers are to be all lawyers, merchants, and land-
lords as heretofore, and that the Association
will raise Sheol when Labour, which represents
90 per cent, of the nation, secures about 20 per
cent, of the voting power. The second is to
“oppose all unconstitutional political procedure,”
which means that the Association will declare all
procedure that it doesn’t like to be unconstitu-
tional, and then oppose it, Then follows axiom
No. 3 : “to promulgate sound views on national
subjects a simple and comprehensive idea, for
every view that' the Association will lose money
by will be unsound, and every view that pays will
be sound; and behind this comes another noble
platitude by which the alleged “Nationalists”
undertake to “prevent undue interference with
the great producing interests of the colony. ” In
other words, The Bulletin presumes that any
interfeience which will make hours shorter or
wages higher will be “undue,” and any interfer-
ence which makes wages lower and dividends
bigger will not be undue. Further, the Associa-
tion undertakes to “uphold a just balance of
electoral representation ’’—presumably so that its
own crowd may have all the representation and
nobody else have any of it; and to bear a hand “in
order that public credit may be advanced, and the
investment of moneys encouraged ” —which is a
truly “National” aspiration, seeing that 95 per
cent, of the nation has no money to invest, and
no credit either. There is nothing in the docu-
ment about helping anybody save the Association;
its distinguishing features are selfishness, hypo-
crisy, blatant Toryism, and an utter inability to
understand that there is anything worth thinking
about on earth save the fat man’s interests. Its
gall is stupendous, as its title sufficiently proves ;
its stupidity is vast and incredible, else it would
have hidden its programme in a cellar and put a
stone on top of it to keep it out of sight, instead
of publishing it in all its naked foolishness. Its
course of action is indefinite, in which respect it
is strangely suggestive of that sort of conspirator
who always begins and ends by writing out his
theories and signing them, and then taking a
huge, red oath of vengeance, and hiring a skull
and cross-bones; its subscription is moderate;
its pres.ent address isn’t known at this office, and
its secretary is named Epps. And that is about
all.

emergency, rode 80 miles (from Bathurst to
Orange, N.S.W.) in 6f hours. The first 30 miles
were done in a shade under two hours.

Which seems to indicate that they are careful-
living men.

The Bulletin hears that it is the inten-
tion of the N.S.W. Government authorities to
release from confinement in Darlinghurst, some
time before Christmas, Mr. G. E. Labertouche,
who, about seven months ago, was sentenced
to five years’ imprisonment for frauds upon the
Imperial Pension branch of the Treasury.
Monday saw Sydney celebrating its annual
Eight-hours’ holiday chiefly remarkable for
the fact that newspapers which are strictly Tory
all the rest of the year on this one day are
violently democratic. However, the workers had
a good time. Thirty-nine societies were repre-
sented, and 10,000 men at least took part in the
procession. Capital spor's were held at Rosehill,
and Labour’s toot was a success as far as any mere
toot can be.
Queensland Government recently decided
to place the gunboat Gayundah in reserve, and
on the last day of September 26 of her crew
were paid off. Acting under legal advice, the
men next presented themselves on board in
uniform, and protested against their dismissal,
on the grounds that they had been definitely
engaged for three years, and that the Govern-
ment had no more right than a private employer
to perpetrate a breach of agreement. Here’s a
how-d’ye-do for the Lawyer-Premier who poses
as a Dembcratic-Radical-Tory.
O’Brien, the Canadian reporter who
alleged that Prince George of Wales and other
midshipmen had been arrested while having a
good time in a low quarter of Montreal, has been
convicted after much “conflicting evidence.” The
Judge, in summing-up, of course said that the.
testimony showed O’Brien’s statement to be
untrue. The Gaiety-girl suicide with which
Albert Victor is rumoured to be indirectly mixed
up, coining atop of Prince George’s alleged
escapades, and the Prince of Wales’ baccarat
business, is awkward for the Guelph blood.
In fear of the future power of the Labour-
party in Queensland some person, whose identity
is kept officially secret, has “objected” to over
2000 of the names that appear on the North Bris-
bane Electoral-roll, each of which names the elec-
toral registrar has marked off, on the formal
ground that he has “reason to suppose that the
person has changed his place of residence.” In
numerous instances, the persons so disqualified
are men who have been born, bred, and all their
lives resident in the electorate. The success of
the Labour-party at the N.S.W. polls has evi-
dently frightened thecapitalistic C.alition Govern-
ment of Queensland.
Apropos the verses, “ Demon Snowshoes,”
in this week’s issue of The Bulletin, we are told
that, at Kiandra (N.S.W.), the champion record
over the snowsbee course (1000 measured yards,
from the summit to the foot of Township Hill),
made five years ago, was 25 seconds—a statement
which is “ hard to swallow.” At Kiandra in the
winter months even the little children.toddle to
school on snowshoes. The shoes in use at
Kiandra are not the Canadian ones, which are for
soft snow, but are identical with the Icelandic
ski, on which a tremendous speed may be attained
over falling ground. This year at Kiandra there
was hardly enough snow for races.
Woman Privilege is coming along with a
vengeance. The wife of one of the Puisne judges
of an Australian province makes constant use of
her husband’s rooms at court, and as she doesn’t
desire to rub skirts with the common herd of law-
clerks and bailiffs who haunt the neighborhood,
she has had a barricade constructed just outside
the Judge’s door. When a limb of the law on a
recent occasion went before His Honor to swear
an affidavit, Her Honor the Judgess took up the
document and read it right through before the
Judge looked at it. It is alleged that the lady
writes out His Honor’s judgments, and thus are
they rehearsed to her before they are delivered in
court. Little surprise would be experienced by
the wigs and gowns, were she one day found in
evidence on the Bench.
The N.S.W. House of Parliament is honey-
combed in parts by the white ant. The library
adjoining the Council chamber has had to be
propped up here and there greatly to the alarm of
the aged councillors ; many of whom are restrained
from clamoring for a new Parliament Ilouse, by
the fear of a land-tax being imposed to meet the
expenditure. The building is a veritable labyrinth.
It consists of three parts, representing three
political eras. The central part was built for
legislative and executive council in the early days;
it is now occupied by the parliamentary library.
The northern wing, that is, the Assembly chamber,
was erected to house the partly elective Legislative
Council. The southern wing in which the
Legislative Council has sat under the present con-
stitution, is a galvanised iron building which was
used as a boarding-house in Victoria in the gold
digging days.

They say that “The Prompter’s Story,”
by J. L. Taylor, now running at Her Majesty’s as
house-wanner to “ Woodbarrow Farm,” hasn’t
been played in Sydney before. Be that as it may,
it is original in idea and gave Albert Bernard,
Laura Villiers’ stage-manager, a chance to show
Sydney folks that he is a capable actor; of the
old-fashioned Cathcart school no doubt, but
powerful and true in emotional moments. Gwen-
doline Floyd, in this as in the piece de resistance,
evinced signs of latent ability allied to good stage
presence, a sweet voice and bright manner.
* * *
It is a great pity that the Villiers’ crowd
didn’t open in “ Woodbarrow Farm,” a pretty
little play containing several unhackneyed situa-
tions —all the more acceptable because unsupported
by stage effects. As Allen Rollit, the young
Devonian who goes to London in response to
the calls that come to him from the great world in
the silent watches of the night and at other times,
Julius Knight has most of the grease-paint, and
uses it in a manly and pathetic fashion, marred
chiefly by his dialect, never Devon, simply a cross
between Scotch and Lancashire. A very fine im-
personation of the garrulous country goody is that
of Emily Stafford, and as the double, the lost heir
and his De Castro, F. P. Stevens gives signs of
ability. Other good performances are those of
Geo. P. Carey, as the blustering, boozing adven-
turer, Colonel Dexter; Mr. Watson, in a smartly-
written sketch of a gambling German baron ; and
Clifton Alderson, in a somewhat minor part, as
the villain whose influence is more apparent than
his presence. Laura Villiers gives a carefully-
ttudied reading of the adventuress who is not all
bad, but aw’fly near it. “ Woodbarrow Farm,”
despite sundry defects, is well worth seeing. It is
well staged ; the farmhouse interior by Bruntonis
a slab out of the heart of England, whose harmony
is outraged by the introduction of a Chinese-made
seven-and-sixpenny “safe” in place of an oaken
cupboard.
** * *

“ Sunlight and Shadow,” by R. C. Carton, played
for the first time in Australia on Wednesday
night, is the story of the unhappy loves of a
choirmaster. It will serve to introduce Marie
Fraser to Sydney audiences. Pattie Browne and
Jenny Watt-Tanner will support her with Messrs.
Titheradge, Lyndal, Bouoicault and Lawrence.
*» * *
“ Potter of Texas” is a four-act drama-
tisation of Gunther’s novel, by George Darrell,
that has not, so far as we know, been previously
played in Sydney city. Briefly, it is a tale of tlfe
rescue of a lady of fortune from the grip of two
Alexandrian Arabs, who desire to kidnap her for
the royal harem, - by an Australian admirable
Crichton. Out of gratitude the lady assists
her hero to recover from his wounds, and, also out
of gratitude, does her level best to ruin and dis-
grace him by estranging him from the girl
of his heart, and showing up his father
as a returned convict. Of course she
doesn’t succeed, for her hoary-headed scoundrel
of an aristocratic papa is ultimately dis-
covered to be guilty of the crime that sent an
innocent man abroad, &c., and the curtain falls
on a scene of peace and triumphant virtue. Of
course, George Darrell is the virtuous and manly
hero—he always is. He was constructed for the
purpose, but it isn't so evident why Christine
Darrell should be the spasmodically wicked
peeress. The comedy, of a somewhat variety
show character, is chiefly supplied by A. G.
Poulton and A. Shine ; and Arthur Greenaway.
Miss Clitheroe, Conny Deorwyn, Geo. Chalmers,
and others wedge in slices of neutral-tinted matter
w’here required. Mr. Wilson’s scenery deserves
special mention, and manager “Dick” Stewart
announces “ Sunny South ” for Saturday.
** * *
“ Cinder-Elleu, Up Too Late,” went on
at Sydney Royal on Monday night. The story
is a little more like a story and less like a
variety show than that of “ Ruy Bias,” inas-
much as the plot is in evidence, according to pro-
gramme, for fully five minutes after curtain-rise,
while the “Roue’s” plot ended with the entrance
of the conductor. Being a newer burlesque than
its predecessor, the jokes in “Cinder-Nellie ” are,
of course, infinitely older. Its music is chirpy
and commonplace ; its puns, archaic; its ballads,
feeble; and its choruses ineffective. Some of its
situations are borrowed wholesale, occasionally
from “ Dorothy ” —and as a work of art it ranks,
musically and verbally, beneath the level of
“Evangeline.” But its costumes are ravishing;
its legs, numerous ; and its dances are entrancing.
The habit dance between Sylvia Grey and
Florence Levey, with its suggestion of horses
pawing and prancing, is a poem in motion,
while that between Nellie Farren and Fred. Storey
is equally clever in the direction of grotesqueness
allied to grace. On the whole, we like Nellie best in

Rice’s “ Corsair,” said to have been a
great success in Newcastle, was produced at
Melbourne Opera House, on Saturday. As a
work of art it runs “ Evangeline ” to a short head,
and it doesn’t come upon the audience with such
a shock. They are educated up to Fortescue, for
instance, and can now regard the circumference
of that monotonous performer without a shudder.
Also Mr. Rice’s original music falls pleasantly
enough upon ears which know exactly what to
expect, whilst the absurdity of the book doesn't
seem unprecedented. The difference between
“The Corsair” and a Gaiety burlesque is repre-
sented by the difference in the two companies.
No money has been spared upon this production.
Well-dressed girls stand around and sing in a
most lavish manner, and when not singing they
are usually dancing a hornpipe. But the job of
making “The Corsair” go is left to the princi-
pals, and the amount of talent that Virginia Earl
can bring to bear upon sucli a task will be quite
understood by all who saw her in “Evangeline.”
Virginia seems to figure as star amongst the
ladies, for Fanny Liddiard’s voice has left her in
the meantime, and George Walton tries hard to be
most funny of the men. Fortunately there is no
heifer in “The Corsair.”

“A Pair of Spectacles’ 1 evidently caught
on, at the Bijou, on Saturday, and the highly
moral tag was listened to with bated breath. A
good start should be everything in the case of
such a charming unconventional little comedy, so
that Anson’s lifelike creation of the vulgar brother
from Sheffield is likelv to become a great favourite
in Melbourne. “ A Pair of Spectacles ” is pre-
ceded by young Boucicault’s sentimental trifle,
“My Little Girl,” but a sudden attack of in-
fluenza prevents the writer from further enlarging
upon the merits of this double bill.

* tit tit *
The tuneful Italians sang “The Barber of
Seville” in fine style on Saturday, albeit their
notion of humour didn’t always meet the require-
ments of the case. On Monday the company, who
seem, by the way, a very happy and united
family, were to give Madame Simonsen a benefit
in “Lucia.” That Madame should require a
benefit is the result of bad luck and bad judg-
ment. It was bad luck that the Cuttica s
insisted on appearing first in unsuitable charac-
ters, and bad judgment that has “mounted”
first-class artists in a fourth-rate style.
* * * *•
The cause of the Cutticas being debuted
in “Martha,” instead of “Lucia,” is explained.
A first appearance in Elotow’s opera was one of
their stipulations in the contract with Madame
Simonsen, who wouldn’t venture to try to per-
suade her prize couple out of an idea that
“Martha” displayed them at their best. Of
course it didn’t. All artists, operatic or other-
wise, are apt to misjudge their “ best, ’ and fancy
themselves in characters they are physically ill-
adapted for.

At Melbourne Royal they intend to put
the Bard away in lavender after Friday night.
Many have paid their respects to “The Merry
Wives ” in the form of hard cash, but few of them
seemed to enter into the spirit of the joke as freely
as the two matrons themselves, whose hearty
laughter began to make them look conspicuous.
In truth, the old comedy has not been assisted by
roars of merriment in front since the opening
night, and without such assistance the plot pro-
vokes comparison with modern efforts, and makes
one seriously consider whether the new kind of
amorous fat man —as usually interpreted by Anson
in this country—isn’t a lot more entertaining than
the extinct species. James Cathcart replaced
George Rignold for two or three nights last week,
but George’s knee is all right again now, praise
the saints ! So on Saturday he will suffer perse-
cution as of yore in “The Lights o’ London.” For
about five years Melbourne has lost its “ Lights,”
and the sight of George giving beans to the evil-
doer in the last act will set many a young heart
beating for the first time, besides restoring faith
in their former hero amongst such as have wept
for Master Ford’s weakness of character.
“lolanthe,” followed by “ Charity Begins
at Home,” has taken the place of “The Gon-
doliers ”at the Adelaide Royal. In “lolanthe”
the Adelaide public are introduced to several
changes in the cast, and for the most part the
alterations are a decided improvement. Flora
Graupner is the Phyllis ; Violet Varley, lolanthe;
Elsie Cameron, the Queen; Charles Ryley,
Strephon; Bracy and Syd. Deane, Tololler and
Mount Ararat respectively ; with Howard Vernon
and Tom Grundy in their old places as the Lord
Chancellor and Private Willis. As usual, Billy
Elton fairly revels as Joe in Cellier’s comedietta,
and receives excellent support all round. The
season continues with a prosperity which brings
gladness to the managerial breast, and when the
double bill shows signs of decay everythiiig is
ready for the staging of Ihe Old Guard.

Oscar Wilde’s hon mot, “An unhappy lion in a den of
Daniels,” has inspired a Parisian manager, who placards
his pantomime bills with pictures of terrified lions, their
eyes streaming with tears, being thrown to Christian
fanatics.
“ Willy ” Walsh writes from Shanghai, where the
Willard Opera Co. had two more weeks to run ere playing
a return season of six weeks at Hong Kong, that every-
body is very well, thank you, and that Cusie Denver(Ada
Hiker) is anything but dead, and that everybody is a
favourite except the baritone, whose voice has faded as
his bank-balance increased. The Willard songsters
visit Singapore after leaving Hong Kong, en route for
India. Friends and unnecessary mourners will kindly
accept this, the only intimation.

The Sydney Banjo and Guitar Society gave their fust
concert at (lie Y.M.C.A. hall on Friday last, under the
guidance of Mr. F. K. Peel. Instrumental music was
relieved by ballad-singing, and the show was a success.
Olga Nethersole, her brother, and Charles Cartwright,
failing to complete expected arrangements with J. C.
Williamson, sailed for England in the Carthage on Mon-
day last. Cartwright has sold the Australian rights of
“The Idler,” “ The Middleman,” “The Profligate,” “The
Ambassador,” “ Village Priest,” and “Fortune of War”
to B. & B. Some of these plays have been seen in only
one Australian city, and there to short runs inadequately
cast, so there should be money in them yet. C.C. will
act as B. & B.’s London agent for the periodical supply of
new plays and fresh talent, hut hopes to return with a
new repertoire and full company in two years on his own
account.

Little Martin Simonsen made a reckless attempt the
other morning to save a whole shilling at one fell swoop.
He went dodging around the unfrequented places of
Melbourne until he found a shop where they sold some
mystery that looked like lime-light material at fourpence
a pound under usual price. Marlin picked up three
pounds of it, as a bargain to go on with, and then flew
back to the Alexandra on the wings of joy and gave a lad
the greatly reduced article to put into the retort.
Fortunately Reims, the mechanist, was on the spot, and
inspected the boss’s “cheap lot” in time to prevent the
laying of the Alexandra in ruins. Simonsen was going to
make his lime-light out of powdered dynamite, or per-
manganate of explosion, or something.

Australia.
The Gaiety Co. are doing in Sydney even better business
than on their last visit. The weekly takings steadily
average £lBOO or so. It is really believed that comic-

The executive of the Tasmanian Exhibition will never
again ask Snazelle to help at a benefit. They were getting
up a show in aid of the “guarantee fund,” and Snazelle'
was induced to give his services for a certain percentage
on the gross takings. The executive didn’t quite under-
stand the difference between gross and net takings in the
show business, but when S. had duly hooked his share,
and the officials had to pass the hat round among them-
selves to pay up the resulting deficiency, they began to
realise the situation. Now, they want to know where the
“ benefit” comes in.

Mr. Lion Margrave, described as “an Australian trage-
dian”—who is he?—made his appearance at the Avenue
Theatre, London, on 26th August in “Othello,” and this
is what the critic of local Daily Telegraph says of the
performance One has to pause a while and think
before one can recall a parallel instance of extraordinary
presumption. Imagine an Othello without one spark of
freedom in his methods, who mumbles his lines yi an in-
distinct monotone. Imagine an Othello like a partially-
washed chimney sweep, whose gestures are those of a
schoolboy giving his first recitation, and whose sugges-
tions of hatred and jealousy are positively laughable in
their lameness and impotence. Imagine this, and you
will have a fair idea of Mr. Lion Margrave’s performance.”

“ Under the Holly,” a cantata composed by the late
J. Churchill Fisher," in the sixties, to the words of R. P.
Whitworth, and performed before the Duke of Edinburgh
some years ago, was produced for the benefit of his widow
last Wednesday night. It is just possible that Fisher’s
later work, “The Emigrants,” of which the words were
supplied by the late Mrs. Heron (“ Australie”), may once
more be brought into notice.

but of late years hotel-keeping at Wellington and Warren
(N.S.W.)died last week at Paddington of cancer. He was
aged 54, and, perhaps, the only man of his profession who
ever became a municipal Mayor, though many of the
mayors are but clowns.
