A young signor of the Simonsen Company has been
struck to the very heart, apparently, by one of the chorus
ladies—a timid, shrinking violet sort of maiden, who
considers the signor is very nice indeed. Every night,
after the operah is ovah at the Alexandria, the gentleman
escorts his little idol home, but seeing that he can’t speak
English, and she can’t whisper soft nothings in Italian,
the question of how they manage to converse is more
than the w'atchers of this romance can master. The most
likely theory is that the happy pair look at one another
and sigh in their respective languages, and finish up with
a gentle caress in Volapuk. The Volapukian kiss, for
instance, speaks for itself and can be answered
accordingly.

Late French papers contain thrilling accounts of Mine.
Bernhardt’s encounter with an iguana in the Australian
wulds. The monster offered a desperate resistance, but
the indomitable courage of the huntress gained the con-
quest. This reminds us somehow 7 of the time when w 7 e
fought 128 rounds with a worm in a dream. Also on
another occasion, when we came home from a banquet
and engaged in a deadly struggle with the doormat, and
the yells of that blood-stained monstrosity were appalling
to listen to, but our unspeakable grit knocked it out after
a two hours’ struggle, in the course of which the air was
filled with blood and fluff and dead mats and other
remains.

the ‘ Midsummer Night’s Dream ’ and ‘Julius Caesar,’ we
furbish up our remembrances of local productions. The
only score of the ‘ Midsummer Night’s Dream’ is at the
Princess’s, with Jefferson as Bottom, supported by Mrs.
Heir, Rosa Dunn, Julia Mathews, Emma Neville, Heir,
Fitzgerald, Lambert, Stewart, Ireland, and Harwood.
Brooke was fond of Brutus in ‘ Julius Caesar,’ and Barry
Sullivan acted it in a sumptuous revival at Melbourne
Royal, the next being for Creswick, Bijou, and the last in
Miln’s luckless lime. The piece has not been seen in
London West since the German Meiningen Co., 10 years
ago.”
Strangely enough, Maggie Moore’s two attempts at
light comedy—“ The Schoolmistress” and “The Late
Lamented”—were made in Mrs. John Wood’s parts. It
was inevitable that Maggie Moore should fail where the
Anglo-American lady had been an enormous success.
Their styles are as wide apart as the poles. Mrs. Wood is
a fashionable humourist, a funny woman who delights in
Paris costumes, and can put on the “frills” of a real
duchess if necessary. Certainly, she used to play burlesque
parts, and sing comic songs once upon a time ; but even
then she w 7 as just the antithesis of “ Lizzie Stofel,”
naturally and artistically. Putting such different extremes
into tights wouldn’t nearly suffice to make them meet —
the extremes, not the tights.
H. A. Jones w 7 as a soft-goods traveller before he set up
for himself as a dramatic author. The best character in
“Saints and Sinners” was the religious old mercantile
humbug that Jones had met in every English town, quot-
ing Scripture as he beat the price down, and snuffling the
doctrine that a person must have “ business energy and
push ” and a black bell-topper to get into the Kingdom.
Push meaning the shoving-over of everybody who doesn’t
leave a clear path tor the good man.

they paint the scenery, pick music, make engagements,
settle business, and then call in the author. Vincent
Crummies’s affair of the Pump over again.

Give him some strong medicine. His tongue is

We have received from Messrs. Marshall
Brothers of George-street, their well-written and
interesting little treatise regarding their popular
tonic preparation known as Marshall’s Tonic
Nervine. Besides a masterly exposition and
explanation of its particular modus operand! as a
health-restorer, it contains numerous independent
attestations to its remarkable merits, by several
well-known and highly respected colonists.
Somebody sends us a thrilling contribution
about the Melbourne Sparrow Club, which seems
to have been doing some tall shooting lately for a
trophy. We merely write this item to ask the
Club if it isn’t darned well ashamed of itself, and
why it doesn’t go and hit something a little nearer
its own size. A woman-walloping association
even would be about 500 degrees less mean and
stupid, for a woman can either hit back or yell for
the police, but a sparrow can’t. The individual
who kills most small birds for a trophy seems, in
our eyes, just about as distinguished as the person
who stamps on most worms for sixpence. If he
would even go about and hit cats with a fire-
shovel we would despise him a good deal less. He
travels, of course, on the plea that he wants to
improve his shooting, but he would get exactly
the same result if the members of the club shot at
each other, and then they would show that they
had at least the courage of their opinions. His
present excuse is just as lame as that of the person
who kicks a blind man from behind in order to
strengthen his legs.

A Bundaberg (Q. ) Rechabite successfully
claimed sick pay on medical certificates for a fen-
weeks. But the medical man discovered that the
joker was “shamming Abraham,” and placed his
next certificate in a sealed envelope. When the
invalid turned up at the Tent as usual with his
little demand, the secretary read out the follow-
ing :—“This is to certify that (Teeantoast)is suf-
fering from chronic laziness. (Signed),” &c.
Sick pay being refused, the malingerer had the
gall to summon the Tent Secretary for it, but the
local P.M. told him that the Friendly Societies’
Act denied him jurisdiction even though his claim
were good.
When Colonel Bob Ingersoll was in Europe
last, he visited Westminster Abbey for the first
time. As he was contemplating the tomb of
Nelson, the guide said : “ That, sir, his the tomb
of the greatest naval ’ero Europe, or the ’ole
world hever knew—Lord Nelson’s. This marble
sarcoughogus weighs forty-two tons. Hinside
that his a steel recepticle weighing twelve tons,
and hinside that is a leaden casket, ’ermetrieally
sealed, weighing over two tons. Hinside that his
a mahogany coffin holding the hashes of the great
’ero.” “Well,” said the colonel, after thinking
awhile, “I guess you’ve got him. If he ever gets
out of that, cable me at my expense.”
Sundry items from one column in one
single issue of the Australian Star :
The alleged Guatemala revolt is all moonshine.
President Barillas denies that there is any revolution
proceeding in Guatemala.
An engagement took place on Wednesday in Guatemala
40 miles from the capital, between the insurgents and the
Government troops.
The President of San Salvador had his troops ready to
assist the Guatemalan insurgents.
General Barillas, President of Guatemala, is making for
the coast, in hope of escaping in one of the vessels.
The New York Herald regards the Guatemala affair as .
a hoax.
The following additional paragraphs weren’t there
—crowded out, most likely :
General Barillas has committed suicide.
It now transpires that there isn’t any individual named
Barillas in existence.
Barillas has entered Guatemala in triumph.
There is no such place as Guatemala at all.
The latest version of the accident to the
German Emperor is that after a good dinner on
board the Hohenzollern the question of strength
cropped up, and the Kaiser, who is said to possess
enormous muscular power in his only available
arm, proceeded to exemplify his strength _ by
taking hold of a heavy iron bar, which he poised
aloft. At the same moment the ship gave a lurch,
and the Emperor, unable to balance himself, fell
heavily and so injured his knee. Talking of
strength, the late mad King of Bavaria was one of
the strongest men in the world. The present
Czar is probably nearly as strong, and Stuart
Cumberland shows a silver rouble which the Czar
bent double between the thumb and forefinger of
each hand. Try to bend a florin and it will be
seen what this means. “ Gussy ” Loftus—son
of the N.S.W. ex-Governor once saw the
Czar, at the request of a lady who wished to
see some of his feats of strength, take a heavy
silver salver and with the utmost ease roll it up in
the shape of a bouquet holder, which he handed to
the fair one as a souvenir.
A correspondent of the Sydney Daily
Telegraph tells over again for the hundredth
time the venerable story of the N.S.W. land
swindler and his swindle; and asks, for the
thousandth time at least, why there is no law by
which that financial carrion bird can be sent
into penal servitude for 15 years, as an exceed-
ingly moderate minimum. Somewhere about the
year 1886 this particular animal bought a few
hundred acres with borrowed money, and piled a
huge mortgage upon them. Then he published the
wonted advertisement, setting forth that this
desirable property was for sale upon easy terms,
extending over five years. The property was
located somewhere on a bright and grassy slope,
with crystal streams scattered here and there,
and the verdure was fresher and more brilliant
than that on any other allotment, and
in general the ground was emerald, and
the sky was azure, and the title was Torrens’, and
everything was gay. The lots were all promptly
sold, six of them falling to the share of a labourer
in the Far North of Queensland. This particular
purchaser toiled for years, and paid up the money
to the last cent; then he sent his wife and family
down to take possession of his farm in this time-
payment Paradise, and prepared to follow them
in person. But unluckily there was no farm, and
no Paradise at all. The vendor had burst; the
estate had been seized by the mortgagee and sold ;
there were no signs of any Torrens’ title ; and the
money was gone hopelessly and beyond all possi-
bility of redress. For, strangely enough, in the
barbarous land of N.S. Wales there is no law that
can do anything to the person who sells a square
mile of mortgaged land to the poor, and collects the
money from the purchasers, and then leaves the
mortgagee to seize the property while he deposits
the proceeds in a safe place and goes broke. The
money has undoubtedly been obtained by false
pretences ; the swindle is a thousand times more
degrading and cowardly than pocket-picking, or
garotting, or plain ordinary housebreaking; but
though there is a statute to give ten years’ im-
prisonment to the shopman who hasn’t rightly
accounted for 18s. od., this particular variety of
infamy is perfectly lawful. There is nothing in
the programme of the “National” Association
about suppressing it. The nominee Upper House
resents any interference with the “sacred rights
of property.” And the nation in general rather
appreciates the fraud than otherwise, for when a
certain politician’s tenants were ejected and ruined
a few years ago under nearly identical circum-
stances, the country left them to drift into the
Benevolent Asylum or any other convenient shel-
ter, but it raised a huge testimonial to the poll
tieian, who softly deposited the same in his hind
pocket and went on his gladsome way in peace.

Jos. Symes does not believe in the news-
paper custom of saying nothing save what is com-
plimentary of the recently-dead. “It is best,” he
says, “to speak of men as you find them, no
matter whether dead or alive. And, in fact, after
death is the only time you can with safety criticise
a Supreme Court Judge. ”

YOUNG MAN: “I have a little thing of my own here - a poem.”
EDITOR (after examining it): “Well, cheap at a sovereign—how does that strike you?”
YOUNG MAN: “That’s really more than I expected.”
EDITOR: “Well, we can’t publish such a poem as that for less.”

A clever German journalist who talks and
writes English at least as well as any man on
the S.M. Herald —mind you, that’s not an ex-
travagant compliment—is at present cook and
barber in a Richmond (Vic.) tobacconist’s shop.
Didn’t get on very well in Adelaide, and now talks
leaders to the occupant of the chair instead of
pitching into the Speaker.
Sir William Robinson was once present in
his gubernatorial capacity at a gathering of S.A.
children. Somebody called for cheers for “our
Guvnah.” All cheered but one school. In-
terrogated as to why they didn’t cheer, a little
girl remarked, “Our mothers told us not to, be-
cause the Governor is a bad man.” And thereby
hangs a delightful little tale.
Mrs. Erench-Sheldon, the African ex-
plorers, in a paper on her travels, says : “There
was nothing left to me but to get an absolute
control oyer the men ; this could only be done by
the application of a raw-hide whip.” Lovely
woman ! And just notice how the adorable crea-
ture drives a horse : flick, flick, flick ! Just as
she drives a man.
When Sir James M‘Bain used to manage
in person one of his north-eastern Victorian
stations, he was asked to act as a steward and to
subscribe to a race-meeting at Oxley Plains. He
refused, on the ground of the demoralising in-
fluence of such sports, but, in order not to dis-
pirit the promoters, “he would allow not more
than four of his station hands to get drunk the
day of the races.”
N.S. W. Railway Commissioner Eddy,
whose salary is £3OOO a year and who has served
the province for just three years, has been granted
leave of absence on full pay. Mr. Justice Foster,
whose pay is £2OOO per annum, who has served
the colony tiiree years and a half, and who is
seriously ill, was at first altogether refused leave,
and has now only been allowed six months.
What does this indicate ?
The late Dr. Singleton, of Melbourne, is
said to have embraced teetotalism in a fit of
disgust with himself. On one occasion he drank
enough Irish whisky to make him sick, and he
“swore off” between the “Ohrni’s.” Such in-
stances of a strong resolution founded on a weak
stomach are by no means uncommon. The non-
smoker is usually a man who lacks the necessary
obstinacy to fight against that awful feeling of
greenness which waits upon the early whiffs.
Taking his income along with his debts
(remarks London Referee) Dolly Tester’s hus-
band, the Marquis of Ailesbury, has £50,000 a
year, or, say, £lOOO a week. A London sand-
wich-man, recently inquested, earned 7s. a week
and tried to set an example to all of us by living
within his income, his weekly expenditure being
6s. 10d., thus apportioned : Week’s food, 2s. l|d. ;
lodgings, 25.; soap, IJd. ; washing, 4d. ; medi-
um, 2d. ; shaving, Id.; boots and clothing, 2s.
President M‘Bain (Sir James) of the Vic-
torian Legislative Council, has made a heap of
presentations in his time to clergymen, “dis-
tinguished visitors,” &c. The only instance on
record where lie didn’t enjoy the process of
“ handing over ” was when Power, the bushranger,
called on him in company with Ned Kelly. The
presentation took the form of M'Bain’s fine horse
and valuable gold-watch. Mac. felt what be
termed the “ humiliation ” keenly. Not theleast
doubt of it. He’s built that way.
Mr. Charles Lett, who, though much his
junior, was a school-fellow of the late W. B.
Dailey, and one of his closest friends, is having a
Portrait of W.B.D. done by the -well-known
pastellist, Hubert Vos, from a photograph which
was specially taken for Mr. Lett when he was
leaving Australia, and of which only one copy
was printed. This new portrait of Mr. Dailey
will probably find its way to N.S.W. National
Gallery.
Sara Bernhardt was disappointed with the
obesity of the Australians who were presented to
her on her first arrival in Sydney. Her conception
of the Australian was a man long, lithe and lean :
it was quickly dispelled by the proportions of the
first score of gentlemen whose acquaintance she
made. Beginning with Harry M‘Quade, her
experience on her first day at the Australia ended
with a well conditioned ex-Minister of the Crown.
“ Are you English, sir?” she said to him. “ No,
madame.” “Ah! FAT, fat, fat.”
The recently-cabled report that William
O’Brien, M.P., would, on his release from gaol,
be “confronted by his first wife ” seems to have
been a canard— with a basis of some sort, how-
ever. O’Brien did make a youthful alliance with a
somewhat giddy person, but, as she has not been
heard of for many years, it is believed that she k
“fad. In his novel, “When We were Bovs”
O Bnen tells a story of a man’s early marriage
and the disappearance of his wife, and it is believed
that from this source, one Russell conceived the
idea of putting it about that Mrs. O’Brien No. 1
was about to appear upon the scene.
At the end of a blind lane off Forest,
street, Bendigo, lies a memento of the local
millionaire quartz-reefer, George Lansell. It jo
the huge cauldron which George used when he was
in the candle-making industry-long before he
acquired his present colossal wealth. At that
tune George used to advertise, “Lansell’s dips
are the best in the market —try them. Better
than imported. ’ At a public meeting, at which
George was present, his connection with the tallow,
tat industry was referred to, and the advertise-
ment read aloud. George smiled, and said he
well recollected the days “when he perfumed
View Point.”
Nobody ever yet found a Melican man
without an initial in the middle of his name but
the surprising thing about it is that very often it
is only an initial and nothing more. The other
day in a Sydney court, the inevitable Yankee was
being sworn, and when he gave his designation as
Hamilton B. Jenkins Fiske (it wasn’t Fiske, but
that will do), the bench wanted to know what the
B. stood for. It transpired then that it didn’t
stand for anything. It was only a lonely initial
wrecked in a howling wilderness of nomenclature
He had been christened B,in fact, just as he might
have been christened Benjamin I O U Smith X
Jones, and that was all there was of it.
Many great men have been habitues of
the celebrated Cogers’ Hall Debating Institution
Strand, London, notably John Wilkes, Philpot
Curran and Dan. O’Connell. Even N.S.W.’s own
lorn Garrett has aired his eloquence there. It
happened thus. During his last visit to London
lorn did not travel on Reohabite principles and
one evening suddenly found himself in the midst
of the Cogers who were engaged in a heated dis-
cussion of the Home Rule question. After a
while, Tom, in whom the cacoethes loquendi is
always strong when he has whisky aboard, joined
in the debate. A demand from the chairman as
to who the deuce he was, only brought forth the
response that they would soon find out. He has
tolerably strong notions on the Home Rule ques-
tion ; so, undeterred by the frowns of the presi-
dent, he plunged into the subject, and made a
speech which, for eloquence and close argument
has seldom been excelled in that hall during the
present generation. Having delivered himself of
his sentiments in vigorous language, Tom, with
the applause of the audience still ringing in his
ears, popped on his hat and plunged once more
into dark space.
We are too frequently disposed to think
of the rapid growth of Australian cities as
merely incidental to the settlement of a new
country, and to regard the European cities as
old and stationary. It is true that their nuclei
are ancient, but so far as the greater part of
their built-up area is concerned, they are
almost or quite as new as the Australian cities.
They, like our own population centres, have
grown unprecedentedly in recent decades as the
result of modern transportation and industrial
systems. Thus, London to-day is five times as
large as it was at the opening of the present
century. From 900,000 at that time, the
population of London grew to 1,500,000 in 1830;
and by 1855 it had increased to 2,500,000.
Since 1855 it has more than doubled. In large
numerically speaking, and closely crowded
cities, where the breathing space is confined,
and where the microbes and germs of disease
are inhaled by every breath drawn into the
lungs, disease gets a foothold and spreads more
rapidly than in open country places. Con-
sumption, Bright s disease, diabetes, indigestion
and liver complaints attack people though, in
all situations, and usually end fatally if
neglected. The first symptoms, as dimness of
sight, tired languid feeling, headache, neuralgia,
Ac., should be cured by regular doses of
Clements Tonic ; this is the greatest and most
successful remedy know' to science, it cures
disease when doctors and their remedies fail.
V itness the following proof :—“ Kangaroo
Valley, N.S.W., Aprils, 1889. Dear Sir,—l
have very much pleasure in bearing witness to
the great value of Clements Tonic in cases of
lassitude, nervousness, and constitutional de-
bility generally. For more than 12 months I
was a martyr to extreme lassitude, loss of ap-
petite, sleeplessness and their concomitant
train of physical troubles, so much so that I
feared I should have to abandon my profession
as a journalist, writing being particularly irk-

POLITICAL POINTS
Reibey, M.H.A. (Tas.), objected to the
Eight Hours Bill because “ time is the working-
man’s capital—that which he uses in the produc-
tion of wealth.” Bosh ! His skill, his brains, his
thews and sinews, constitute his capital,
B. D. Morebead has boasted privately
that he will drive MTlwraith out of public life.
Morebead might save his teeth from gnawing at a
file. Mac.’s physical want of condition needs a
venomous opposition as a restorative tonic.
A picnic committeeman at Bourke, N.S
Wales, the other day asked the bandmaster to
play “ God Save the Queen.” “Don’t know it,”
was the reply. “Wha-a-t!” “ And we ain’t
a-going to learn it; that’s more. It’s played out.”
Grey is “an orator, a scholar, and a
patriot,” but Parkes is only “a great figure in
colonial politics ” in Christie Murray’s judgment;
and the Australian republic is inevitable, “ owing
to the vanity and the courage of the Austra-
lians.”
The growing tendency in a Socialist
direction is, indeed, the most remarkable fact in
current Liberal politics, says the Penny Illus-
trated. All the young men nowadays call them-
selves Collectivists. Ten years ago they were all
Individualists.
It is the fun of the world to see a division
in the N.S.W. Council. The old dodderers can’t
remember one another’s names, can’t hear answers
to their queries concerning them, and don’t know
how to spell them when they painfully arrive at
the information.

because “it might be the means of returning to
Parliament an Irish Catholic majority.” In other
words, that fine old democrat David Symc fears
“ Let Labour and Capital united be ” in
the same individual.

affirmed that the island shall enter any federal
union of four provinces which includes N. S.
Wales and Victoria ; so that it will stand out as
long as N. S. Wales stands out.

St. James Gazette is enthusiastic about
Bruce Smith, the N.S.W. Treasurer, but says that
he holds the opinions which belonged to the
philosophical English Liberal of 20 years ago.
After this, B.S. will surely consider the advisable-
ness of obtaining a set of up-to-date politics.
The Masters and Servants Act which the
N.S.W. democratic party wishes to repeal, was
carried by old Jack Robertson in the face of a
powerful squatting opposition in both houses; it
was regarded as an ultra-democratic measure in
its day. “ The clock of the world moves on.”
Dobson, who leads the Tasmanian Oppo-
sition, has recovered a farthing’s damages from a
political Scotchman for publishing an electioneer-
ing skit in which sordid avarice and unpatriotic
conduct were imputed to the legal firm of Dobson
and Co. The touchy attorney called five lawyers,
three parsons, and a moneylender to prove the
libel.
Sydney Echo says that “the modern
democrat is a man who, conscious of his mediocrity,
is anxious to drag all above him down to his own
level.” And the modern aristocrat wants to shove
everybody down below his own level, because he
lives by being on top, and he can’t climb worth a
cent.

drank 6,231,733 gallons of liquor, an increase for
the year of 340,000 gallons. Beer increased from
2,895,259 gals, to 3,204,895 gals.; of native wines
50,000 gals, were drunk, an increase of 10,000 gals.
An average of gallons to every man, woman
and child in the province.

Of the “six points” of the famous
People’s Charter, which Sir Henry Parkes advo-
cated with all the enthusiasm of his nature in
1838, four points will soon be embodied in the
laws of N.S. Wales. “Universal Suffrage ”is
being brought within the sphere of practical poli-
tics; but on the remaining point, “Annual Par-
liaments,” the old man is silent.
If the Labour members in the N.S. W. Par-
liament want to abolish the running of parliamen-
tary trams and trains, they should table a motion
requiring the Commissioners to furnish on the
first sitting day each week a statement of the cost
of the “special ” trams and trains for the previous
week. The weekly return would soon arouse the
taxpayers from their listlessness.
The absence of a large section of the
N.S.W, Parliament at the Melbourne Carnival
may give Parkes a chance to snatch a vote on the
Commonwealth Bill. A promise should be ex-
torted from him not to raise the Federation-under-
the-Crown bogie during the Cup week. The
Federal Council Enabling Bill was defeated by a
majority of one or two only on Cup Day 1884.
St. James Gazette calmly says that the
N.S.W. Labour-party largely consists of the most
ignorant, brutal and fanatical type of Irish dema-
gogue. That the most of the Labour representa-
tives are not Irish either by birth or blood will at
once be seen from their names. However, The
Bulletin is rather glad to see such statements
printed. Appeal to religious prejudice is Toryism’s
last card.
Burgeon Creed, M.L.C. (N.S.W.), voted
against Langwell’s bill abolishing that obnoxious
labour-conspiracy law of George IY. “because
nothing was put in its place.” Asked if he
considered it necessary to put anything in the
place of the tumours he excised, he replied:
“No, but you might mistake'9, gland for a
tumour.” j.M.C. evidently considers the George
IY. Act a gland.
When the annalist of 1891 endeavours to
discover the causes of the financial panic in
Sydney, he will be egregiously in error unless he
includes in the list the Hon. William M‘Millan.
Had the N.S. W. ex-Treasurer dared to oppose the
Building Society interest in Parliament two years
ago, his bill to regulate the Societies’ procedure
would have become law, and only the shoddy
organisations would have gone bung.
The political virtues of Parkes’ pernicious
Public Works Committee are being rapidly recog-
nised by Australian politicians. It enables a
Premier to command the support of a number of
needy members; it prolongs the life of a Ministry;
it seduces the roads and bridges men from their
party allegiance ; and it demoralises the constitu-
encies and the houses of the legislature. What an
apt instrument in a Premier’s hands !
New York Standard says “ it is unwise to
prejudice the Single-tax cause by presenting the
Single-tax as gospel. Preachers can easily present
the scheme from a Bible point of view.” The
Bulletin undertakes to present any scheme under
Heaven, from pitch-and-toss to boiling your
grandmother, from “a Bible point of view.”
Eight words in the Bible have sacrificed the lives of
seven millions of people, mostly women. The
words are : “ Thou shalt not suffer a witch to
live.”
Remark by “ Sammy ” Tomkinson, of S, A.
Legislative Council: —
Land is the foundation and source of wealth. (Hear,
hear.) The earth is the mother of us all. (Hear, hear.)
Therefore, land ought not to be taxed at all. (Cheers.)
We proceed to extinguish this rash assertion in
one act. If the earth is the mother of Tomkinson,
then the earth’s name must be Tomkinson also,
for names are hereditary. And the earth’s name
isn’t Tomkinson. Therefore, the whole legend
falls through, v
The Bulletin has evidence that book-
binding work is done inside the N.S.W. gaols for
schools of arts, judges, magistrates, and lawyers,
at less than half the cost at which it would be
undertaken by the trade ; and that the vans of a
well-known “big” tradesman are to be seen
leaving Darlinghurst each week with the products
of convict labour. The mats and matting are sold
by the authorities at prices much below those for
which similar goods could be made by free-labour,
but there is no difference in the cost to the con-
sumer, the difference being pocketed by the store-
keeper.
Under Jawbone Neild’s Bill (N.S.W.) “for
the Suppression of Vice,” owners or lessors of pre-
mises converted into houses of ill-fame are liable
to a penalty of £lOO or six months’ hard. Why
not apply this principle to the rich owners of
Chinese gambling-shops? It is nonsense to say
the owners cannot be found. If they cannot, their
agents can be rendered liable, and if there should
happen to be any difficulty about identifying the
agents, then let the State collect the rents from
the Chinamen and hold them till someone comes
forward for his gruel. “Six months’hard !” How
that would make certain N.S.W. Legislative
Councillors sit up!
The junior bar have a tight grip on one
Government billet in Sydney. The leader of the
Government in the Legislative Council is allowed
a secretary, as no salary is attached to his port-
folio. The secretaryship was vacated recently,
audit has again been bestowed upon a very junior
barrister. A manly Civil Service Board, heedless
of the frowns of a Minister, would not sanction
such an improper appointment. Many an intel-
lectual man in the Civil Service would be glad to
have the soft billet, as he would then have a
chance to study law or medicine. But the post
is reserved for junior barristers, an Act of Parlia-
ment to the contrary notwithstanding.
Parkes is going to “favourably recom-
mend” an expenditure of £200,000 of loan-money
on N.S.W. roads, and after that there will only
be £700,000 left, out of the £4,500,000 just
borrowed. The people of the country districts
will feel grateful to Parkes for this, and some of
them will vote for him in consequence. In
Queensland, Victoria, and elsewhere the divi-
sional boards or shire councils look after the
roads, and consequently the people are not under
the necessity of feeling grateful to any politician
every time a rut is filled up or a culvert mended.
That is the exact reason why Parkes has hung fire
with his Local Government Bill for 25 years.
When it is passed a great hunk of patronage will
be gone, and r-oads will no longer bring votes or
gratitude either, and Parkes lives on roads to a
great extent.
In an editorial, the Sydney D.T. says :
If the Upper House is not to have the free rigfit of
reviving such a measure as the Crown Lands Act Amend-
ment Bill, a measure in which no question of taxation is
involved, it has no right to touch legislation of any kind,
and referring Bills to it is a pure waste of time
So it is, brother, a perfect d.t. This opens up the
whole question of the value or uselessness in a
democracy of a Second Chamber—a little prickly
hedge which the D.T. falls crash into, by sagging
on like this
By the deliberate voice of the people (!) however, that
right is conferred on the Council, to be used, presumably
in' s the country’s interest.
Now, when was “the deliberate voice of the
people ” asked anything at all about the Legis-
lative Council —the Hospital for Incurables among
political paralytics—its powers and its privileges?

Society
The story goes that the venerable Judge
Josephson got married the other day for the
fourth time) settled £30,000 on his young bride.
* # #
The Judge aforesaid must be verging on
80 years, yet it is—or was, until very recently—
his custom to eat every morning for breakfast hot
bread right out of the oven, and eggs boiled until
they were as hard as the hob of Gehenna. Which
little peculiarity was the source of the only trouble
he ever gave any hotel-keeper on circuit.
* # *
General Booth says that “ reporters work
so much they haven’t got time to be saved.”
That’s just where Booth is wrong. Reporters
work so much they haven’t time to be lost. It
takes a man with a lot of leisure to be lost.
« * *
A Southern magnate, who died the other
day, left the money he had intended for his son to
the divorced wife of that son. This was done at
the instigation of the latter’s mother-in-law, who,
revenge being sweet to woman, now glories in
finding herself the mother of a woman worth
£30,000, and in seeing her wicked ex-son-in-law
and his yellow-haired fascinator without a cent
to jingle on the family tombstone.
• « *
A young man who has just returned from
England to N.S.W., brings to his father, a well-
known official, a copy of the Apocryphal Peerage,
as well as a new edition of the Herd Book. This
will obviate any more absurd mistakes in regard
to distinguished visitors.
* * *
A will dispute recently settled in an Aus-
tralian court, was over property acquired by the
investment of the savings of the maternal head of
the house, who in her young days worked like any
man deep down in the coal-mines.
# * *
Fin de siede ad. (literal extract) from
Sydney D.T. :
r ADY HOUSEKEEPER, musical, good cook and
manager, 12 months in first-class family, could
take charge of bachelor’s or widower's family. Gov.
Institute, 108 Bathurst-street.
And, after all, why not ? Isn’t it just about time
that the bachelor s family were acknowledged ?
What is it that George Eliot says about bachelors’
children in “ Silas Marner?”
* » *
Who is that unblushing snob of “ a
colonial lady in Europe ” writing chatty letters to
the Argus occasionally ? Her strong point seems
to be her husbands. Some of them are dead, and
one is still living, and the way the lady prattles
on about my present husband, my last husband,
my other husband, and my husband before that
one, is the only redeeming feature in her genteel
vulgarity.
* # #
A certain Victorian Civil Service enquiry
now proceeding should open the eyes of the C.S.
to the fact that the reign of one crew of officials
is just about as bad as the reign of the other.
Apropos, the other day a private communication
for Mr. So-and-So got into an official envelope.
Bulked with other correspondence, it was opened
in the usual way, and ran : “Dear , Young
Friedfish, in your department, is a sou of our
esteemed and worthy brother in Masonry,
Brother Friedfish, &c. If you can, &c., &e.”
He could and did. Friedfish got six months’ back
pay and a rise of twenty pounds over others on
the same footing.
* • #
Alderman Jeannerett says that the Sydney
milk companies weekly throw 1000 gallons of milk
into the harbour to keep up prices. Forty or
fifty years ago John Tawell, chemist, of Sydney,
afterwards hanged in England for murdering his
paramour, similarly disposed of many hogsheads
of rum. Ergo, we have rum-and-milk in our
beautiful harbour. Q.E.D.
• # *
Dr. “Diamond” Beaney, of Melbourne,
in making his will appears to have forgotten that
he had many relatives in Canterbury, Eng., who
are in very humble circumstances. A nephew of
his, as a matter of fact, recently applied to an
Australian magnate for employment as under-
butler. It seems a mockery that the Doctor
should have wished to have his virtues recorded
on a monument in Canterbury Cathedral whilst
members of his family were on the books of the
board of guardians. “Diamonds” left £lOOO to
the Cathedral, conditionally on a £l2OO monument
to himself, for which he provided the money,
being erected in the building. The Cathedral
authorities are in a fix, for whilst they
don’t want to lose the “cool thou.” they are
still more unwilling to allow the monument
to be built. A compromise is proposed by
which a mural tablet would be placed in a very
dark corner.
Frank Barnes, of Young Australia,
announces in his paper that Lady Jersey has
offered four prizes for plain needlework, and on
looking them over we notice that two of them are
for night-dresses, and the other two are for speci-
mens of another garment which we are too bashful
to mention. Lady Martin, some time ago, gave
prizes for night-dresses and the other thing in the
same way ; and when Lady Oarington started out
to encourage plain needlework, she also offered
rewards for the same old night-dress, and the
other article which we have endeavoured to indi-
cate. And now The Bulletin would like to
know how Frank Barnes introduced the topic to
vice-regal notice, seeing that he is a bashful
and embarrassed youth, and not at all fitted to
tackle the subject in a plain, bald-headed fashion.
* * *
A rather prim Sydney man the other day
entered a toney local restaurant near lunch-time
and took a seat. Opposite him was an eminent-
clerical potentate from an adjoining province,
who was alternately biting a scone, sipping tea,
and making copious notes, probably for a sermon,
in a massive book. The rev. gentleman having
exhausted his provender and ideas took out of his
pocket a comb which he carefully ran through his
whiskers, hair, and eyebrows. Having done this
he carefully cleaned the comb with his fingers,
shook the debris into his scone-plate, and, mutter-
ing something, probably grace, left: the shop.
And so did the other man.

#' # #
Ex-school-teacher Young, of Albertan (Q.),
who was recently committed for trial at Been-
lelgh on a charge of wife-murder, interrupted his
recent second-marriage proceedings by asking
Parson Hausmann, the celebrant, to brace himself
up with a drink. And the other day in England
a bridegroom was heavily fined for having, in the
middle of the marriage-service, helped himself to
a generous snifter from a bottle of spirits with
which lie had provided himself to counteract his
natural nervousness. The parson married him all
the same and collected the fees, but afterwards
prosecuted him for sacrilege, or something.
* * *
Now that the Sydney Ladies’ Club has
been granted permission by Premier Parkes to
picnic and boil its billy on the Sydney Botanical
Gardens lawn the local Tailoresses’ Union is pre-
paring also to boil its little billy there.
# # • *
Mr. Thomas Robertson, solicitor, a man
of far more than average professional ability, has
died at Hay, N.S. W., aged (il. In the early days
of The Bulletin he brought a libel-action against
this paper and recovered the substantial sum of
£lOOO damages and costs (amounting to a good
many hundreds more, which, as the case was con-
ducted for him by his law-partner, must have
turned him in quite a handsome little lump).
There is no harm in confessing that the verdict
was a proper one, although it is quite certain that
Mr. Robertson could not have suffered a penn’orth
of damage from the allegations made, inasmuch as
they were so thickly veiled that the reference was
unrecognisable even by his own intimates until he
personally brought the article under their notice
with a view to making them witnesses. It
is easy to be charitable with other people’s
money, and when Mr, (now Chief Justice)
Darley, who was Mr. Robertson’s counsel,
said that his client, who appeared to
vindicate his character, and did not come to the
Court with an empt> <ocket and an itching palm,
would devote to charity any sum he might be
awarded by the jury, the latter rose to the occa-
sion and piled on the damages. The Bulletin
has never been able to find out, though it has
made a number of applications on the subject, to
what “charitable purposes” its little thousand
sovereigns were applied. It has heard from time
to time vague rumours that part of the charity
took the form of a very ugly daub (probably
worth, with frame, £7 10s. in a good market) said
to have been painted by a relative of deceased,
which was presented to N.S.W. National'Gallery;
also someth!ngSEbout a window presented to some
church or other, but it fails to recognise any
charity in these matters. In justice to the
memory of the deceased lawyer it would be well if
somebody let the world know how the money
really was applied.

The champion mean man turned up in
Sydney the other day. He got married at 11 a.m.
and spent the rest of the day calling with his
bride on no' less than seven printing establish-
ments getting tenders for 25 wedding-cards,
« * #
The Baroness De la Zouche (at least she
used to be the Baroness De la Zouche before De
la Z. divorced her a year or two ago) has in Lon-
don raised the old question : “ Drunk or Sober.”
Two policemen deposed that she was fearfully and
wonderfully drunk while riding in the park and
was behaving herself in a manner dangerous to
human life. The house surgeon of the hospital to
which she was taken on arrest emphatically swore
that she was drunk when admitted, and that he
galvanised her into sobriety. On the other hand,
one or two fashionable doctors who saw her some
time afterwards said that there were no signs of
drink about her. And so the charge was dis-
missed.
• • »
A drunk when galvanised properly, at once
becomes marvellously sober, and the electrifica-
tion of boozers is the choicest variety of (German
compound) medical-student-sport. You see a
whiskyfied gentleman carried into the casualty-
ward in an utter state of drunken oblivion, so
completely obfuscated that you can open his eyes
and tickle them with a feather without the
patient objecting in the slightest. Then you take
his boots off and begin galvanic operations on his
soles. Jumping Jehosaphat, how it does make
him sit up ! The fashionable London doctors who
were called in to depose that Lady Zouche could
not have been drunk, stated that galvanism
"was not, under the circumstances, proper treat-
ment for a lady.” Anyone who has seen the
wicked medical students take off a drunken lady’s
foot-gear with a view to scientific operations, will
fully appreciate the humour of the situation.
* # #
Rev. Father Somebody, preaching in
Sydney suburbs recently, assured his hearers that
“ Divorce is a modern institution, an outcome of
the modern atheistical spirit.” The rev. gentle-
man is wrong. Divorce is about as modern nn
institution as babies, or sin, or The
earliest reported divorce, as per Mesopotamia
Times and Advertiser file at this office, was in
the days of Abraham, who filed a petition against
Hagar, the lady who started the Prohibition
Society in the wilderness. The only thing modern
about divorce is our way of doing it. Of old, a
hearty and aggressive kick was the only recog.
nised rule nisi, and if anybody objected or gave
further trouble, the rule was made absolute with
a meat-axe. Nowadays we run crowded courts
and co-respondents, and female witnesses who saw
through the keyhole, and other things which are
fearfully and wonderfully made. The enlightened
evening paper, too, reports the utterances of the
said female, and tells how she looked and what
she saw, and what she didn’t see, and why she
didn’t see it; and describes the clothes that the
petitioner had on, and the haggard grin of the co-
respondent. Nobody reported what Hagar, the
great original respondent, had on ; in those days
you simply heard a row in the next tent, and
when you looked out the divorce was finished.
But the principle itself is contemporaneous with
the primeval ape and the prehistoric saurian, and
the first divorce took place when the primitive
Aryan man came home to his tree, and found that
his wife had gone out on all-fours to wink at the
missing-link next door.
* # •
Last Friday Gov. and Lady Jersey visited
Tiger Inglis’ Sydney tea-emporium, and there
was nearly a column about it in one of Saturday’s
daily papers. Governor Jersey is nearly down to
the bottom of things at last. The lowest depth of
all is that which Carington reached when he wept
over the burnt bedding-factory, and Jersey doing
duty as a tea-advertisement isn’t far off it. By,
and-bye the Earl will begin taking maimed foot-
ballers home, and there will be items in the
papers telling how he found a cabman and his
fare swearing over a disputed shilling at midnight
and how, like Carington, he nobly got out and
made peace by paying the shilling himself. After
that he will commence to pat neglected infants on
the head, and to embrace sticky babies. The
affable Governor always goes downhill in just the
same way.
# * *
The collapse of the Y.D.L. Bank has
brought disaster upon many old Tasmanian
families, such as the Facys, Murdochs, Fishers,
Crouches, Crisbs, Gracies, Farmilos, Arnolds,
and Haywood s.
# * #
When anything “ fetching ” is on at
Sydney Centennial Hall, it is interesting to con-
trast the wild rush of the enthusing crowds for
the seats they pay for in the Hall, and the calm
indolence of eastern gallery invites as they stroll
in to get seats which they don’t pay for. The
fiction is that the gallery in question is reserved
for “distinguished visitors,” of whom 400 or 500
are supposed to be continually out of gaol and in
evidence. Even if the fiction were a fact, this
wholesale deadheading would be pretty rough on
the paying public, who own the Centennial Hall,
but the eastern gallery crowd never are to any
large extent “visitors” to Sydney, and they are
not, as a rule, “distinguished” for anything
except chilled-steel cheek and a fixed determina-
tion to get everywhere for nothing.
# # #
Sydney deadheads are the same at every
gathering. At last oratorio among the D.H.’s
a suburban bailiff was to be seen sitting near an
aspiring pork-butcher and a celebrated local
chronic insolvent, and the only one in the crowd
who ever answered the alleged description was
a gen'leman who was a “ visitor ” some years ago
under Government auspices, and was then “dis-
tinguished ”by a number. The aristocrats who
cadge invites at Government House, billets in
Macquarie-street, free-passes to theatres, and free
feeds everywhere, are equally successful at the
Centennial, and that is why the “distinguished
visitors” there assembled have such a homely
aspect. Being under municipal control, it follows
as of course that the Hall must be heavily dead-
headed ; but, as a matter of favour, we implore
Mayor Manning to try and raise the average
respectability of the city’s guests : if there must
be society loafers in the eastern gallery, let them
be clean loafers and not lewd fellows of the baser
sort. Or, if this improvement cannot be'effected,
it would be some relief if a new set of distin-
guished “visitors” were occasionally allowed to
replace the inveterates who manage to go to 300
entertainments each per year on a total expendi-
ture of Is. 3d. for tickets and tips.

other day in Sydney Hospital. Why a common-
place occurrence like this should be categorised, as
phenomenal is not apparent. The writer knew a
Queenslander, years ago, who rejoiced in the
A SONG.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The sun may shine, the rain may fall,
And the world roll round about, —
The king’s men and king’s horses all
Can never rub one thing out.
Skies may darken—clouds will flit—
Troubles may gather and go:
For my sweetheart loves me “just a bit 1 ”
And, oh ! I love her so.
The vapour vanishes in the sun !
So pass cures, doubts, and pains—
For I’m “ loved a bit ” by the dearest one,
And the best the world contains.
The Breaker.

sobriquet of “ The Wild Scotchman,” who used to
eat glass whenever he was on a stirring jamboree,
and, after he had tapered off horrors with plus
the d.t.—two distinct and utterly opposite stages
of alcoholism, the writer can assure you on his
experienced and professional honour—his thateli
would bristle with the jagged spines of broken
glass that jutted out all over his skull, and he
could lacerate a whole tribe of naked niggers by
charging them bull-fashion with his spikev head.
He didn’t need trephining; neither did he die of a
pane in his skull. The poor fellow who pegged out
in the Sydney Hospital, had probably been dining
on soap-boiler’s waste, seasoned with lime, and
topped off with seaweed. Going recklessly to
sleep with his heated head resting on a wet bag ot
sand some of the particles of alkaline waste, lime,
and seaweed flew to his head ; chemical action
was superinduced between the now sizzling sand
absorbed into his porous skull and these atoms,
and glass was the resultant. His alcoholic dreams
bein" naturally of the bottle, mind triumphed
ove/matter, and psychologically caused the glass
to begin to form itself into the shape of a popular
pizen-holder. Unfortunately, he awoke before
more than the neck had been manufactured—
awoke with a head and a thirst on him before he
could complete the bottle and fill it at the usual
morning pub. The skull, in reactionary disap-
pointment, decomposed, and he was compelled to
turn up his toes. Such incidents are finite common
in the sandy districts of Australia; they are
simply the natural result of a sequence of very
ordinary causes.

And here is another young woman, aged
19, who has poisoned herself with strychnine.

It is now regarded as a fortunate day in
Brisbane when there are less than five insol*

* * *
A Melbourne gent, named Cribb was sen-
tenced to 12 months’ imprisonment the other day
for having curling-tongs in his possession. The
tongs were so “ faked ” as to make them suitable
for “ removing goods at a distance, - ’ and although
Cribb protested that he only carried them for his
’ead of’air, the downy Panton, P.M., refused to
bite. In point of fact, the man’s name was very
much agin him, Cribb !
# * *
Some men have all the luck. A man from
the bush lately came to Sydney, put up at the
Exchange Hotel, went to the races, and got “on
the spree.” One morning he dropped out of his
pocket £3OO in notes. The precious bundle was
found on the floor of his bedroom by a housemaid,
who honestly returned it to him and was rewarded
with £5. That was lucky, wasn’t it? Well, two
days afterwards the poor man died and now he is
buried. Some men have all the luck.
* * *
A daughter of Jack Woods, M.P., pub-
lished a novel a few days ago, and now he (Jack)
is honeymooning in Sydney.
# * *
How they do love lords, and fleets, and
snobbery, and things in Melbourne ! The demo-
cratic spirit in Victoria appears to be sadly on the
wane, as if the Southern province had expended
itself during the Berry outburst; while in N.S.
Wales the Nationalistic cause waxes steadily.
Perhaps, however, the fervour of the Victorian
welcome to the latest accession to the British fleet
in these waters is in some measure an outcome of
the indifferent reception accorded it in rival Syd-
ney. Sydney regards Melbourne with good-
natured toleration ; Melbourne views Sydney with
sublime contempt.
* * *
A Bathurst (N.S.W.) undertaker has in
his employ a cheerful young man who, the other
day, went out while drunk to deliver a coffin. He
forgot the number of the house, and, bearing his
ghastly burden, knocked at every door until he
found the right one.
* * *
The other day Pastor Grubb—a shrieking
brother just out from England—held a holiness
meeting at Geelong (Vic.), and scooped the pool in
a way that the pool was never scooped before in
Australia. He told the citizens of the black trans-
gressor who was caked with dirt by the Gaboon
and the ancient Niger; and the brown lost soul
who wallowed in the swamps of the Gambia, where
squashy insects with eight legs hang out, and fat
reptiles perspire amongst the slush, and gigantic
bugs straddle through the ooze and slime; and he
told them so many other saddening tales that the
audience was all flabbergasted and broken up. It
was so badly broken up that Grubb’s hearers rose
and heaved their watches and jewellery to the
preacher, and dashed their purses and pocket-
books on the platform, and some of them
wrote cheques for the naked heathen, and
some made their wills in favour of
that undressed savage, and some volunteered at
once to go forth and preach to him. Grubb had
to hold them with both hands at last to prevent
them giving their clothes and boots and hats to
the lost nigger right there on the spot, and
scudding home with nothing on ; and for an hour
or so these auditors wallowed in a trampled mire
of tears and mud and cash, and ran over each
other and broke each other’s backs in the mad
struggle to get at Grubb and give him the cash.
And yet, when the visible or concrete heathen—
the local beggar, or the Chinaman, or the nigger
selling brass jewellery—came round next morning
there was no enthusiasm about him, and nobody
even remembered that he was really the same in-
dividual who aroused so much enthusiasm the
previous night.
## ‘ •
The Australian Anglican General Synod has
recorded its “firm conviction that any extension
of divorce, such as has been allowed under the
Victorian Act, is inconsistent with the Divine
law. A suggestion : Place the sole power of
divorcing (as well as of marrying) in the hands of
the clergy, with thumping fees attached, and
the “ Divine law ” will probably speedily accom-
modate itself to the vox populi.
* * *
By the rigid canons of legal etiquette, a
client cannot consult a barrister without the inter-
vention of a solicitor, but Barrister Canaway, who
appears for the N.S.W. Minister for Lands at the
Land Court, takes his briefs or instructions direct
from officers of the Lands Department, without the
aid of any solicitor. As barristers are pretty
touchy upon etiquette, it is strange that the
N.S.W. Law Institute does not take official notice
of Mr. Oanaway’s “ poaching.”

* # *
The oldest bank building in the Australias
is, perhaps, the Derwent Bank, to be seen behind
the publishing office of the Hobart Mercury.
The name of the old banking-house can still be
deciphered on the front wall: it stands some 50ft.
back from the street, and in its palmy days it
was within a stone’s throw of Government House.
# * *
The wicked old men who sit in the front
row of the stalls and ogle Tottie will take kindly
to the views of Mr. Fox, the distinguished thea-
trical wig-maker. Says Fox “As in the bar-
barous times men were more hairy than now, so I
maintain that in the future, with greater refine-
ment and education, men will be almost if not
quite bald. ”
In a certain Sydney suburb there was a
girl dying, the other day. She had been ill for
some weeks, and the usual host of sympathetic
friends had called round in the usual silent,
muffled, soft-footed way, and inquired about her
in husky whispers at the door, and then they had
shaken their heads, and hove a long sigh apiece,
and gone away. One of these regular inquirers
Was a young woman whom the family failed to
recognise, but she seemed so sympathetic that
they set her down as a school friend of the patient.
One day, however, they told her sadly that there
was no hope, and instead of going away she
stepped softly into the hall, and wiped away a
tear, and seemed as if she was trying to
speak, but couldn’t do it owing to her
emotion being too much for her. Then she took
something out of her pocket and pressed it with a
long, lingering, affectionate pressure into the
mother’s hand. It was a card, and on it was the
legend, “ John Smith, undertaker, 948 -7 street;
funerals conducted at lowest rates.” I am so
sorry for you,” said the visitor in a sad, sym-
pathetic tone; “if you’ll give a trial I’m
sure he’ll give you every satisfaction.” And then
she breathed another long sigh and trotted away.

Premier Parkes declines to give any further
particulars concerning the enterprising lady who
the other day obtained from him the use of the
most beautiful part of Sydney Botanic Gardens
for a private party, beyond the fact that she is
“the daughter of a well-known public man.
The assumption, common among politicians, that
the sons and daughters of public men are and
should be privileged, here obtrudes itself.
# # *
The Anglican Prelatic Conference lately
sitting in Sydney has been eating ham and poul-
try with its Friday teas. Someone bewails the
fact in the S.M. Herald, whereupon Zachary
Barry, LL.D., lays down his merry thought and
harabone to explain that fasting and abstinence
is not ordered by the church, but is a purely
voluntary obligation.

Dear Bulletin, —Be your valuable re-
marks anent the inequality of sentences passed at
Sydney Quarter Sessions, here are two extra-
ordinary cases before Judge E itzhardinge.
Murray, found guilty of stealing a purse, contain-
ing a few shillings, from a lady’s pocket (first
offence), three years. Robert Mathieson, guilty
of breaking, entering, and stealing £BO worth of
jewellery (very little of which was recovered, the
prisoner refusing obstinately to assist the police to
that end), two years in an easy gaol (Dubbo).
One is led to imagine that the sentences vary
according to the condition of the Judge’s liver or
something.—Yours, etc., H.V.A.

The dirtiest outsides —backs —at the Public
Library symbolise the dirtiest insides.
Common-sense has got the thin end of the
wedge into Spain. The recent flood cablegrams
stated that dead human bodies were becoming an
intolerable nuisance, but the dead cattle were
being cremated. A nation is rapidly advancing
to the great inevitable funeral system when it
commences to burn the defunct cows.

Duncan Gillies recognises woman’s superi-
ority over man “ in certain ways,’ but doesn’t see
that she is qualified to vote. Woman’s proper
sphere is at home, says that nice old gentleman.
Which being the case, we rise to ask bachelor
Duncan when he intends to provide somebody
With a sphere of her own, where she can prove her
superiority in certain ways ?

He kept a house which was no better than
a bagnio, and made a fortune out of it. And
when he died, his sorrowing relict went to his
solicitor, one of the executors, and asked him to
suggest an epitaph. The man of law wrote on a
sheet of foolscap, “ He wore the white flower of a
blameless life ” —which is now on his tombstone.
* * #
The other night a Melbourne youth was
going peacefully homeward along the St. Kilda-
road, when he was roused to sudden activity by
the screams of a girl some little distance ahead.
It was dark and the road was lonely, but the
young man instantly dropped his cigar and rushed
wildly to the rescue. He shot round the next
corner, and there clinging to a lamp-post was a
distracted maiden with her hat damaged, and a
cascade of glossy brown hair rolling down her
back, and a look of dreadful alarm in her large
bright eye. Behind her was an enormously fat
man with two chins, and a disorganised bell-topper,
and a bald, red head that was smoking with
excitement, and a huge red face; and he was try-
ing to climb over his white waistcoat and his
vast stomach and kiss his captive. He was just
about to succeed when a fist arrived in his eye
with a squashy sound like a dumb-bell hitting a
bad melon, and as he let go with a yell he got an
awful biff in the other eye, and one on the ear
that rolled him over. Then the victor gave him
a fearful and tremendous kick, and after that he
took the aged reprobate by the pants and the
collar, and scrubbed him up and down in the
gutter. When he had finished this exercise he
escorted the frightened girl home, and had a good
time generally. It was nearly two hours later
when he got to the paternal mansion, and the
first thing he saw on going in was his own old,
bald parent in an evil plight. He had beefsteak
on both eyes, and his head was swelled so much
that he needed a No. 18 hat, and he had both his
feet in hot water, and a cooling lotion on his
stomach. Also, he had lost two teeth, and con-
sequently was relating with considerable difficulty
how he had fought with 18 larrikins —and left
four of them insensible in the road —in defence of
a helpless girl.

“ General” Booth shys he has fallen head
over heels in love with Melbourne. Now, a man
who loves Melbourne cannot love Sydney, any
more than a man who loves London can love
Pai'is. Booth, perhaps, -is like old Billy Hoskins
on benefit-night; “Dear friends, I love your city
and hope to leave my bones here.” Consequently
150 Australian towns are entitled to Billy’s
bones.

# # #
Two of the most interesting monuments of
Australian discovery —that at Albury, to com-
memorate the passage of the Murray by Hume
and Howell, and that at the marked tree on the
Blue Mountains —are each disfigured by an inac-
curate inscription.
# * *
A Tasmanian individual has got into a
certain amount of trouble for cremating his dead
infant in his back-yard. He had a conscientious
objection to burial, and there were no local
facilities for cremation in the ordinary sense of the
term, so there was really nothing for it save to
combust the body with kerosene and fire-
wood. As there was no other statute to meet the
case he was fined for unlawfully burning rubbish,
and being a common nuisance.

RADAM’S MICROBE KILLER.
9 Smith-street, Balmain, N.S.W.,
11th August, 1891.
To Radam’s Microbe Killer Company.
Dear Sirs, —I have been a great sufferer for over
sixteen years from pains in the legs from the hips
down to the toes, and also in great misery for
nine years from a kidney disease, during which
time I found I was losing the use of my legs,
particularly the right one, from weakness in the
joints. I tried nearly every known remedy, and
received but little relief until I commenced to use
the MICROBE KILLER.
It is now eight weeks since I commenced its
use, and having used two one-gallon jars, I find
myself an entirely different man. I believe a
little more will entirely cure my complaints, as I
can say that I have not felt so well for eight or
nine years, and can now do my work as well as
ever I could, and also enjoy my meals, which for
many years I could not. —I remain, yours thank-
fully,
THOS. FISHER

This chair adjusts itself to any position, from

VAGARIES OF MACISTRATES
hear a great deal from time to time of the
* * vagaries and eccentricities of Judges and
Magistrates, and of late the matter has been, not
infrequently, the subject of heated and acrimoni-
ous debate on the floor of the Legislative Assembly.
Chamber. Purity and sound judgment in thd
persons of those who are empowered to administer
our laws are to be desired above everything if
a country is to avoid reproach and ridicule. The
expression, “Justice’s justice,” has become a bye.
word, and conjures up many unpleasant memories.
Perhaps one of the best stories in this connection,
and one which has a humorous side to it withal,
comes from New Zealand. It is an old story, but
a true one. A certain Northern Justice of the
Peace, who resembled necessity in that he “knew
no law,” was called upon to adjudicate in a case,
preferred against a man whose “looks were agin’
him. Almost before the evidence was concluded
the sapient J.P. exclaimed, “Three months ; take,
him away; his face is enough to condemn
him. 1 Fortunately the then Provincial Council
of Auckland happened to be in Session,
and the same evening a member brought
the matter before the Council, and moved
an urgent address to the Governor to release the
man at once. The way this member carried his
point with the Council was unique. He glanced
round the chamber impressively, and said, “I
should like to know what honourable member
will be safe, if people are to be sent to gaol for
having ugly faces.” That fetched them. It was
an argumentum ad hominurn that was irrefutable.
The motion was carried unanimously, and the
man with the disfigured physiognomy released.
It seems that the unfortunate victim of the
Justice’s stupidity had acquired the peculiar
expression of countenance, which so nearly proved
faFal to him, as the result of a painful facial
affliction, from which he had suffered for years,
unconscious of the knowledge that a proper use
of St. Jacobs Oil would have relieved his suffering,
and restored to its pristine beauty the deformed
index which was the involuntary cause of his
trouble.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
'ilie inaugurating shriek of the racing Car-
nival ” will be beard on Saturday (10th) at Caulfield, after
which day the very air of Melbourne will smell of stable,
and any creature resembling a tout will be regarded with
'silent awe as be leans against a lamp-post with a straw
protruoi ig from his facial chasm. Suurday is Guineas
jjjjy, the great annual occasion when people who “know
something” can sometimes get good odds against it, and
those who know nothing have to buy their information
as they go along. The unexpected happens with great
regularity in this annual dawning of the Carnival, and
“ form ” (the last year's) is upset as violently as the stalls
at a Beauty Show. Consequently no racing card of the
season is fuller of melancholy interest, after the event,
than that which commences with the Caulfield Stakes.
The Stakes is worth considering, inasmuch as three great
performers—Mentor, Dreadnought, and The Admiral
have won it in the last three years. Such good ones as
Bungebah, Marvel, Megaphone, Tirailleur, Cuirassier, and
Malvolio are among the present entries, and if the last-
named should make a vigorous show against one or more
of the top-sawyers be will look like a rather moribund
fowl for the Melbourne Cup right away. All the likely
V.B.C; Derby candidates are entered for the Caulfield
Guineas, too, so it is more than possible that the betting-
market, such as it is at time of writing, will be turned
inside out on Saturday, along with the pockets of a good
many punters.
* # «
Bookmaker Bill Branch, who died the other
day in Melbourne, was a most impassive gambler. It is
related that a friend of bis once noticed him camped
asleep with his pockets turned out, and awoke him with
the intelligence that lie had been “gone through,” point-
in'’’ to the inverted tell-tales. “ Oh, it’s all right,” mur-
mured Bill, “I was having a camp after losing every
blessed cent. I turned my pockets inside out to let any
passer-by see that I wasn’t worth investigating.”
# # *
Ere joining the turf the late “Bill” Branch
was usually occupie 1 in “seconding” fighters in the
prize-ring. lie seconded Joe Kitche iin his big fight with
black Harry Sallars, a fight which lasted four hours, both
me.i being blind at the finish. Sallars. unquestionably,
was the belter man of the pair, but, being a stranger,
Branch and his friends bustled him out. Sallars had a
benefit given him at the old Prince of Wales in Lonsdale-
street, on which occasion the giant Black Perry—who had
unfortunately killed a man in fair fijglit—put on the
gloves with Tom Curran, but after the first round took
them off and could not be induced to put them on again,
although he had shown to advantage.
# # *
Said Branch; “At hazard, backing the ins
wilt strip you to your shirt, and backing the outs will
strip you to your boots.”
# • *
Pugilist Fitzsimmons, writing in a ’Frisco paper,
sneers at Hall who, he says, is not eager to fight. He
despises H ill, whom he can beat as sure as the sun shines.
“ Why shouldn’t I lick him ? I licked him twice before
already. lam cleverer, bigger and more experienced
than when I beat him years ago, and there is no reason
why I should not win from him now.” Fitz. offers to
fifht Hall, betting him 6000 dollars to 5000, the battle to
take place in some secluded spot with ten witnesses on
either side, or he “ would agree to go into a room with
him for two dollars a-side without a soul but ourselves.
Any style of fighting that suits him will suit me.” Fitz-
simmons says bis heart is set on fi filling Pritchard, of
England. Corbett, on the other hand, is enthusiastic
about Hall, who, he says, is the bdst boxer he ever sparred
with, and would beat Fitzsimmons.
# # •
A new American yacht, a real flyer by all
accounts, has just demonstrated to the Yankees the fact
that deep-keeled yachts are more than a match for centre
hoard boats. This vessel, the Gloriana, a 46-footer, has
for the eightli time shown her heels to the fastest boats
of her class, including the well-known Beatrix, designed
by Burgess, of Volunteer fame.
# # 9
There was quite a big Austral an show at the
recent Goring (Eng.) regatta. Sir James Garrick has a
house on that charming part of the Thames for the
season, and he had a large number cf guests. The
challenge fours fell to a crew of cornstalks, the two
Garricks, Ned Lord, and Drury, a Bananalander. In the
dongola race (a race for punts propelled by eight paddles,
two ladies and two gentlemen, each side) Bailey
Macarthur and fils sister Miss Enid Macarthur took part.
Their crew was second. The Garricks also won the pairs,
so that altogether Australia did well.
* * *
When llenuiker Heaton occupied a responsible
position in connection with Sydney Evening News and
T. and C. Journal, a very severe paragraph appeared in
the sporting portion of one of those papers reflecting in
no measured terms on the manner in which a boat,
belonging to Sydney Want, had been managed in some
race or other. Next day, Mr. Want, accompanied by a
friend, entered Henniker’s sanctum, demanding very
warmly the name of the writer of the offensive par., and
threatening no end of pain and penalties ; he wound up
by giving Heaton twenty minutes in which to make up
his mind either to give up the name of the writer or take
the consequences. As soon as he had gone Heaton put
on his hat and went off to tell the writer of the paragraph
what had happened. “By all means,” said that gentle-
man, “tell Mr. Sydney Want that I wrote what has
so offended him." Shortly after Mr. Want if
possible more irate tjian ever. “Well,” said Heaton, it
is not usual to divulge the name of the writer of anything
which appears pi ouy paper, but in this instance I nave
the writer’s permission -to do so. Mr. J. 11. Want,
barrister-at-law, wrote it." “Come along, Tom, ’ said Mr.
Sydney Want to his friend.
* * •
The English Rugby footballers’ share of the gate-
money at the Cape was £7OOO.
« * *
The Argus dished up the result of the Lan-
cashire Plate along with an extraordinary outrage by a
hand of armed men in San Antonio. The three placed
horses in the Plate figured as the name of the ringleader
in the outrage—Signorina Orme Martagon. It would
take the Argus a long while to dash off such a good joke
wilfully.
# # •
In reference to Bookie Bill Branch, a histori-
grapher recalls his old friend, Leviathan Slack, a per-
fectly ignorant man, who used to fill his betting-book with
Egyptian hieroglyphs, and translate them to little Ward,
the barber, his secretary. Slack’s wife kept a Metho-
dist Chapel going in Prahran, so that it shut up when he
had gone.
* * *
Branch figures in clever Lascelles’ well-known 50
little caricatures of the ring, the lurking satire of which
they could never perceive, but hung them in the place of
honour at Tattersall’s.
* * *
One of Roberts, the champion billiard-player’s
daughters is quite an artist at the game, some of her
strokes being up to the old man’s form. An excited
admirer, the Other day, wanted to back her against
any player in South Australia, Miss Roberts to con-
cede 500 in 1000, spot-barred. Nothing came of it,
however.
#* . #
A Melbourne youth was fined 20s. and costs the
other day for using obscene language at a suburban race-
( meeting. We don’t find a zealous constable running-in
any member of the ring for expressing his feelings when
the favourite wins,
* # *
People never before heard of in Sydney senior
cricket are now much heard of in the press on the subject
of the proposed trip to England. The writer cannot see
what objection there can be to a party of cricketers
doing a tour in England, provided they do not pose
under false colours as Australian representatives.
• • •
There is in English waters a yacht named the
Neptune, which has won over 1000 prizes.
# * *
Yale and Amherst (U.S.A.) University pro-
fessors of athletics assert that non-smokers have from
42 to 66 per cent, more chest-girth, and 8.36 cubic inches
more lung capacity than smokers. Hum ! So much more
chance, then, to accommodate and breed the tuberculosis
maggot. Smokers can’t be scared into weed-abandon-
ment that way.
• * #
Larry Foley avers that it is untrue, as stated,
that he ever thought of offering a scrapping benefit to
Postmaster-General O’Connor. Also, he points out that
when he interfered in the Dora Wiley trouble, mentioned
in a recent issue of The Bulletin, he did so purely in his
capacity as lessee of Sydney Gaiety Theatre.
# * '•
Some people deride the idea that Pritchard
should challenge Joe Goddard, but the latter tsuwtastilg
or strong nor one whit cleverer than the Tipton Slasher
whom Tom Sayers, no bigger than Pritchard, beat. Of
course weight counts for more in a glove than in a ring
fight, but against this we must remember that Pritcham
made short work of Jem Smith, who at one time had a
reputation second to none, and quickly settled Jack Burke.
# # •
All sorts of yarns were rife last week as to the
quantity of cleverness Jack Ashton had concealed “ up his
sleeve,” but after the American’s showing with Joe
Goddard in Melbourne on Friday night it did not appear
as if there was much truth in the rumours. Joe, as per
usual, started to plough his way clean through his man,
and it was only by ducking and clinching that Ashton
managed to survive the stipulated number of rounds.
The Yankee was seconded by John L. Sullivan, but even
that did not frighten Joseph. Ashton cut by far the
sorriest figure of all who have faced Goddard, forChoynski
and Doolev, and even little Billy M’Carthy knocked the
tornado down before their strength was worried out of
them.
# • •
Mick O’Brien, the well-known jockey, has gone
to Melbourne from Hay, much improved in health through
his trip to Riverina. O'Brien’s trouble is asthma, and if
any place on earth will relieve asthma it is Hay. A
man known to the writer had suffered from asthma from
boyhood, and at seventy years of age he visited Hay and
stayed there, for some fifteen months. He never again
was troubled with the complaint and lived till nearly
eighty.
• • •
Mr. Dougharty, the Melbourne boxing enthu-
siast, has offered to add £2OOO of his own “ stuff ” to any
purse offered to bring Sullivan and Slavin together, pro-
vided the match comes off in the Melbourne Athletic
Club ’ * #
“Sullivan is the coming champion sculler of the
world,” remarked an authority on these matters the other
evening- “ But what about Stansbury?” “ Sullivan can
beat Stansbury with ease,” was the reply, “ and if Searle
were alive and at his best I would put the pair in the hat,
and just as soon draw one as the other.”
# * •
Ohoynski, the game, gentlemanly and clever,
but scarcely heavy enough American fighter, was ban-
queted and‘benefited in Sydney ere his return to America
in the Alameda. He leaves Australia with the good will
of all.
After all, David Christie Murray had some
excuse when he said there was much bad language
used in Australia. He probably listened to the
people blaspheming on the front steps of the hall
as they came out from his lectures.
The Theatre Royal Hotel, Sydney, has
lately changed hands, and is now under entirely
new management. A splendid stock of wines,
spirits, liqueurs and cigars has been laid in, and
an altogether new feature is provided in the shape
of an American bar, where all the strange, curious
and refreshing drinks known in the United States
can be obtained. Their name is legion, but among
them may be mentioned champagne cocktails,
gin fizz, milk punch, mint julep—probably the
noblest liquor known to man—imperial punch,
Manhattan cocktails, and many others. All
mixed by American bar-keeps. James A. Walsh,
proprietor, late of the Occidental Hotel, San
Francisco.*

If ever we get lured aboard an Italian
vessel, we will sing the hymn “For those in peril
on the sea ” with much fervour. Also, whenever
we are sailing along the middle of an ocean and
happen to hear of an Italian vessel approaching in
the distance. The gaiety of a life on the bounding
wave seems to be getting eclipsed by Italian
vessels.

“A GOLDEN SHANTY.”
The Demon Snow-Shoes.
(A LEGEND OF KIANDRA.)
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I he snow lies deep on bill and dale,
In rooky gulch and grassy vale,
The tiny, trickling, tumbling falls
Arc frozen ’twixt their rocky walls
That grey and brown look silent down
Upon Kiandra’s shrouded town.
The Eucumbene itself lies dead.
East frozen in its narrow bed,
And distant sounds ring out quite near,
The crystal air is froze so clear,
While to and fro the people go
In silent swiftness o’er the snow.
And, like a mighty gallows-frame,
The derrick in the New Chum Claim
Hangs over, where, despite the cold,
Strong miners seek the hidden gold.
And stiff and blue, half-frozen through.
The fickle dame of fortune woo.
Ear out, along a snow-capped range
There rose a sound which echoed strange,
Where snow-emburdened branches hang,
And flashing icicles, there rang
A gay refrain, as towards the plain
Sped swiftly downward Carl the Dane.
His long, lithe snow-shoes sped along
In easy rhythm to his song ;
Now slowly circling round the hill,
Now speeding downward with a will;
The crystals crash and blaze and flash
As o’er the frozen crust they dash.
Among the hills the first he shone
Of all who buckled snow-shoe on.
For though the mountain lads were fleet,
But one bold rival dare compete,
To veer and steer devoid of fear,
Beside this strong-limbed mountaineer.
’Twas Davy Eccleston who dared
To cast the challenge, “if Carl cared
On shoes to try their mutual pace,
Then let him enter for the race,
Which might be run by anyone—
A would-be champion.” Carl said “Done.”
But not alone in point of speed
They sought to gain an equal meed,
For, in the narrow lists of love,
Dave Eccleston had cast the glove,
Though both had prayed, the blushing maid
As yet no preference betrayed,
But played them off, as women will,
One ’gainst the other one, until
A day when she was sorely pressed
To loving neither youth confessed,
But did exclaim —the wily dame,
“ Who wins this race, I’ll bear his name.’
These words were running in Carl’s head
As o’er the frozen crust he sped,
But suddenly became aware
That not alone he travelled there,
He sudden spied, with swinging stride,
A stranger speeding by his side;
The breezes o’er each shoulder toss’d
His beard, bediamonded with frost,
His eyes flashed strangely, bushy-brow'd.
His breath hung round him like a shroud.
He never spoke, nor silence broke,
But by the Dane sped stroke for stroke.
“Old man ! I neither know your name,
Nor what you are, nor whence you came;
But this, if I but had your shoes
The championship I ne’er could lose.
To call them mine, those shoes divine,
I’ll gladly pay should you incline.”
The stranger merely bowed his head—
“The shoes are yours,” he gruffly said;
“I change with you, though at a loss.
And in return I ask that cross
Which, while she sung, your mother hung
Around your neck when you were young”
Carl hesitated when he heard
The price, but not for long demurred,
And gave the cross; the shoes were laced
Upon his feet in trembling haste,
So long and light, smooth-polished, bri, lit.
His heart beat gladly at the sight.
Now, on the morning of the race,
Expectancy on every face,
They come the programme to fulfil
Upon the slope of Township hill;
With silent feet the people meet,
While youths and maidens laughing greet.
High-piled the flashing snowdrifts lie,
And laugh to scorn the sun’s dull eye,
That, glistening feebly, seems to say—
“When summer comes you’ll melt away :
When I grow strong you’ll change your song
I think so, though I may be wrong.”
The pistol flashed, and off they went
Like lightning on the steep descent.
Resistlessly down-swooping swift
O’er the smooth face of polished drift
The racers strain with might and main.
But in the lead flies Carl the Dare.
•Behind him, Davy did his best,
With hopeless eye and lip comprn sed ;
Beat by a snow-shoe length at nun t
They flash and pass the winning-post.
The maiden said “ I’ll gladly wed
The youth who in this race has led.”
But where was he ? still speeding fast,
Over the frozen stream he pass’d,
They watched his flying form until
They lost it over Sawyer’s Hill,
Nor saw it more, the people swore
The like they’d never seen before.
The way he scaled that steep ascent
Was quite against all precedent,
While others said he could but chose
To do it on those demon shoes ;
They talked in vain, for Carl the Dane
Was never seen in flesh again.
But now the lonely diggers say
That sometimes at the close of day
They see a misty wraith flash by
With the faint echo of a cry,
It may be true, perhaps they do,
I doubt it much, but what say you ?
