The Second Mrs. Jowker : A Bush Sketch.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The tin-mining township of Piligi was
once a lively place, they say. This shows what
deceitful things appearances are. A visitor to
Piligi at the present time—if there happened to
be such a person, which there never does—would
come away, as speedily as possible, with an undy-
ing conviction that the place ever was, is, and
will be the abode of utter desolation, rusty fish-
cans and empty nothingness. There is a road
passing through Piligi, but it is never used except
by a stray Chinaman —who comes in to sell 6pb.
of tin to the publican—or a mob of predatory
goats out on a foraging expedition for gunny-bags
and jam t'ns. This road bisects Piligi, and just
one-half of the town used to adorn each side of it.
The pub. occupies one side of the street, and on
the other there was, until lately, a composite
structure of bark and gin-cases, which was
alleged to be a store. The stock-in-trade was
principally sardines, and a purely malignant kind
of tobacco; but sometimes there were several
bottles of salad-oil on the shelves, as well as a bag
of sugar and some kerosene and a box of candles
under the bunk in the back roqm. This was the
emporium of Mr. Thomas Jowker, who, with the
publican and his family opposite, constituted the
population of Piligi.
« « #
Jowker owned two rectangular beasts dis-
tinguished as horses, also a blue-painted wag-
gonette with four odd wheels and a stringy-bark
pole, lie used to tie his two quadrupeds to this
machine twice a week, and disappear over
the creek into the depths of the bush,
in a frantic endeavour to sell something
to the miners who were supposed to haunt
its recesses. I believe he did find a man
sometimes, at the bottom of a hole, but the per-
centage of man per hole was small, and it is a
wonder Jowker did not get discouraged when he
used to drive up to, and look down shafts all day
without finding anyone. Ahd*»vhen he did discover
an underground toiler, he almost always met with a
liscourteous reception, and was told to go to
fophet out of that with his bloomin’ old rattle-
trap. And when Jowker’s beasts wearily'hauled
the chariot homo again, Jowker would be told by
the publican that there was a regular procession
of would-be customers in bis absence, and tint
they had all gone away swearing at Jowker for
not being at home. Put when the unlucky mer-
chant did stay at home, the customers did like-
wise. • It was only on the occasions of Jowker’s
pilgrimages into the bush that people were seized
with a longing for Jowker’s merchandise,
* # # ■
Realising that his inability to remain at
Piligi and hunt up diggers at the same time, wafi s
causing him to suffer heavy cash losses and
depreciation, Jowker soliloquised. After a long
tune spent in consideration, he engaged an old
derelict fossicker of his acquaintance to mind the
shop wh le he—Jowker—was engaged elsewhere.
Harry was to receive free board and lodging and
half-a-puuud of the malignant tobacco weekly fur
ms services. Put 1 tarry proved to be a man of
deceit; for whenever he did sell any of Jo.wker’s
goods' he straightway meandered to the pub.
opposite and remained there, as long as circum-
stances— and funds —permitted. Then when
Jowker returned in the evening Harry reported
no sales.” Put Jowker missed the goods which
dis unjust steward had disposed of, and he also
detected a powerful rum-aroma mingled with
Harry’s breath, and therefore he fired Harry out.
# # *
This last disaster strengthened an impres-
sion that Jowker had had for a long, long time.
He would have to get married again. For there ■
had already been a Mrs. Jowker. She had
died partly through Jowker’s ill-treatment, and
partly through starvation—Jowker’s disposition
being very frugal. It was this element of
penuriousness in his character which had hitherto
prevented him from taking another partner—“ a
woman do eat such a of a lot,” he would say.
But he now determined to invest in a helpmate
as an economical and prudential measure. She
surely would not eat as much as that rogue Harry
used to drink.
So, one evening the blue-painted wagonette
brought a fresh inhabitant to Piligi. She was an
athletic woman, with a firm jaw and a brisk
moustache; and Jowker made it known that if
satisfaction were given during a week’s trial of
domestic virtues, he intended to bestow his hand
and heart upon this lady. This bargain had been
cheerfully agreed to by the prospective bride, who
entered upon her housewife duties at once. The
week of probation passed rapidly; . the new r
arrangement had proved highly satisfactory, and
on Jowker’s next visit to “ town ” he was accom-
panied by the muscular female, who returned
with him to Piligi as Mrs. Jowker No. 2.
# # *
For the next few weeks there was nothing
but peace, and joy, and love in the Jowker house-
hold. Mrs. Jowker cooked her husband’s meals,
she washed his clothes, and she attended to the
shop when he was away. She chopped wood,
drew water from the creek, and heaved rocks at
the goats. She reared a crop of chickens, another
of ducks, and had a few young turkeys in various
states of impaired health recuperating in the
grateful warmth of the camp-oven. She adorned
the shop window with a deftly-arrayed assortment
of mixed goods, judiciously varying salad oil and
pickle bottles with samples of soap and bacon and
copy-books. Altogether Mr. Jowker was delighted
with the result of his experiment.
But one evening when he came home, Mrs.
Jowker was not there to bid him welcome and
hobble out his horse as usual. There was a sound of
revelry in the bar of the pub. opposite, and Mr.
Jowker was not long in discovering that it was
chiefly caused by Mrs. Jowker. The one weak
point in that lady’s character was displayed at
last. Mrs. Jowker was “on the guzzle ” !
# * *
Jowker had encountered many specimens
of hard language in his time, but the hardest of
them were but mere baby-prattle to the unsancti-
fied expressions which now proceeded from Mrs.
gJowker’s mouth. She made disparaging remarks
personal appearance. She in-
sulted the"’memory of his ■. ancestors for many
generations aud briefly apriounced‘ her
intention of laying 6m ’with -ar'i"J>ottle‘'as
soon as she had time. ,Tl%ii she.uttered a long
series of demoniac shrieks, flni% a tumbler at the
publican, and |ay down on the floor to drum her
heels on the boards-aiid sing “ Willy Reilly.”
Jowker ,was almost paralysed with surprise.
- But -vvlien he entered his home lie became seriously
angry as well. The interior of the place was quite
disorganised ; kerosene and sardines and tobacco
strewed the floor which was besprinkled with
flour. Thirteen fowls with dislocated necks were
on the bed. The remains of the bedding were
smouldering at the back of the fireplace, and
Jowker’s entire stock of crockery lay in fragments
on the ash-heap. The only part of his premises
that had escaped disaster was the front shop win-
dow. Here, behind the four pane window-sash
were seven bottles of salad oil and a ham which
Mrs. Jowker had evidently overlooked.
Jowker prepared his own evening meal that
night. He had crossed the road to remonstrate
with Mrs. Jowker, but she was asleep at the
time, and when he endeavoured to arouse her she
struck him heavily with a bottle. So Jowker
returned home and decided on a “lock out.”
At 1 a.m. he was aroused by Mrs. Jowker’s
demand for admittance. Her language was unduly
coarse, and Mr. Jowker refused her request. Mrs.
Jowker increased the intensity of her talk and
subsequently went round to the front. Here she
poked out the four panes of glass with her
umbrella and raked down the seven bottles of oil
and the ham with one sweep of her nervous right
arm. The crash started .1 owker out of bed, but
he was only in time for an indistinct vision of an
active female fleeing towards the Creek with an
umbrella and a ham amid coruscation of swear
words that startled the very goats.
Jowker had hardly got to sleep again before
another explosion of language aroused him. He
sallied forth this time with a pick handle, but was
not agile enough to get within range of the des-
troyer of his rest. She fled across the creek with
the velocity of a frightened wallaby, and crashed
through the scrub on the other side with a series
of intermittent yells. Jowker stubbed his toe
badly and limped home swearing. But Mrs.
Jowker was there almost as soon, and fired stones
into the premises through the broken windows,
and the performance was continued with un-
flagging energy until morning and the opening of
the pub.
# # #
Jowker saddled up one of his steeds in
the morning and went into Bogalong for a police-
man. When the trooper arrived at Piligi, Mrs.
Jowker was engaged in bombarding the hostelry
with large rocks. She had been expelled by the
landlord because her supply of cash did not cor-
respond with her desire for liquor. She had then
broken into her late residence and carefully
gathered the remnant of Jowker’s stock in her
apron. These she threw down a shaft. Then she
had fired Jowker’s store and performed a war-
dance round the blaze. After this she had filled
in the intervals of cursing the publican by
breaking his windows.
It took the united efforts of Jowker, the publi*
can, the “trap” and a pair of handcuffs, to
coerce Mrs. Jowker into taking a seat on the blue
painted waggonette, and when it departed, bound
for Bogalong lock-up, peace once more brooded
with outspread wings over the goat-haunted
village of Piligi.
■lf
The magistrate awarded Mrs. Jowker
“two ; -months’ hard.” When the time had
expirfed, Mrs. Jowker “footed it” to Piligi, full
of repentance; ami a desire to “ make it up ” with
Jowker, and live happily ever after. But
Jowker had left for parts unknown, and had left
no address for Mrs. Jowker, and in place of her
former home there was nothing but an aching
void.
She has not yet discovered where Mr. Jowker
abides, but is still hopeful. She roams the
country with great patience, and all the treasure
of a woman’s affection will be showered on the
absent Jowker whenever he chooses to let it
shower.
But I think Jowker will maintain a strict
neutrality.
A. C
In the Transvaal, under a new law, nobody
can wear a wig without a license, or dye his hair
whiskers, moustache, or eye-brows; and ladies
with “fronts,” or false curls, or artificial tails of
hair that have been bought in a shop, must be
registered. The penalty is a fine of £lO or 14
days’ imprisonment.
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.
One of the few industries which have
survived and prospered in N.S. Wales, despite
Freetrade and other disadvantages, is the great
piano and sewing machine business of Messrs.
Beale and Co., of Sydney, which is travelling
ahead with surprising rapidity. At Annandale,
in the immediate vicinity of the city, Messrs.
Beale have constructed a splendid factory for
the manufacture of sewing machine frames and
cabinets, and all the other ornamental wood and
iron work connected with their business. This
valuable industry suffers under the iniquitous
disadvantage of having to pay heavy duties upon
most of the raw materials it consumes. A duty
of 201 per cent, is exacted on the spirits of wine
largely used in polishing. Goods finished by the
foreigner may be imported absolutely free of duty,
whence it follows that such an enterprise, re-
munerating local talents and labour, is heavily
fined for doing so. In face of this stupid injustice,
an immense trade is doing in the decora-
tive parts of the business. The woods employed
are almost entirely Australian, and the timber of
the Australian forests, in the hands of the skilful
workmen employed, has been proved far superior
to the vaunted productions of the South American
and other foreign woodlands. Unfortunately its
is but little used—in this matter, as in many
others, distance lends enchantment to the view,
and the far-off article is valued merely by reason
of its remoteness. Vast quantities of timber, far
more beautiful than the costly material imported
from the further side of the Atlantic, are burnt to
make a bonfire for the ignorant selector, and but
for Messrs. Beale’s untiring enterprise much
more would meet the same fate. The
quantity used in their factory is enor-
mous, and a vast array of the finest machi-
nery enables them to turn out work which would
do credit to the best workmen of London and
Paris. Several of the most valuable machines are
of Messrs. Beale and Co.’s own construction, and
being used exclusively in their factory, they place it
even ahead of its foreign competitors. The firm has
now moved into new offices, above its central show-
rooms, 484 George-street, Sydney, and as the pre-
mises are fitted-up exclusively with Australian
woods, they furnish a fair sample of the
boundless resources of the forests of this con-
tinent, and the capacity of Messrs. Beale’s work-
shops. The Australian, in some senses, is a
modest individual. He imports his bishops, his
military men, his railway commissioners, and all
his high-priced public officials ; but when he takes
to importing his timber and his ready-made
cabinet-work—so long as Beale & Co. are above
gi-ound-his sanity Iv comes doubtful, for the
advantages of efficiency and beauty are all with
the local article.

‘John M‘R.” (Sydney): Hardly good enough
iO print, and yet too good to make us long to attend your
funeral “W. Kiniber”: We object to railway men
having to work on Sunday unless they get a day off
during the week to make up. If they' do it is all right.
Every' man has a right under present conditions to one
day’s rest in seven, or two if he can get them—which day
doesn’t matter a straw. But if you want to stop all kinds
of Sunday work you may as well sit down with a sickening
thud and give it up at once ...“ Robert P.F.”: Your
thrilling story about a man who washed his head in the

The melancholy event in the late Judge
Webb’s life was the death of his favourite
daughter, from small-pox, at Bombay, while they
were on a pleasure trip to England, and Webb
was so greatly attached to her that he bore the
marks for the rest of his life.
Any Bill to regulate the management of
Building and Investment Societies in iV.S.W. will
be a sham and a snare unless it (1) fixes (as in
Victoria) the proportion of a Socit ty’s borrowings
on deposits and debentures to its paid,up capital
in cash; (2) does not compel the discrimination in
the periodical balance-sheets between realisable
and such non-realisable assets as flotation ex-
penses, good will, office-furniture and stationery;
(3) does not likewise enforce distinction between
capital nominally paid-up and actually paid-up;
and (4) does not make it a criminal act of the
directors and managers to include as an asset an
inflated and fictitious value unless by special
resolution of shareholders in general meeting
assembled.

The Roumanian Royal Family appears to
have been suddenly broken up and done for by a
sweet young brunette,’ with two violet eyes and a
most delightful dimple. -This-destructive vision,
one Mdlle. Vacarescu, was pet maid-of-honour to
the Queen when the royal eldest son and heir—
the Tummy of those parts—fell violently in love
with her. The King swore a good deal over this
impolitic attachment (very likely he himself had a
sweet tooth for the maid-of-honour), but the
Queen, who ,is a giddy, romantic old thing, per-
suaded him-to give his consent to an “engage-
ment.” Thereupon the Ministry appealed to the
country to decide whether an Heir Apparent
ought to marry out of his royal “spere,” and the
country, being idiotic, said that sijfh a thing
wasn’t to be thought of. -Then/#.e yeigilhg
family commenced to cottapsg. First of®lH the
Heir Apparent, Prince Ferdinand, went away to
Austria, or somewhere, to hide his grief. Pre-
sently the Queen grew hysterical and departed for
a distant land, taking 1 the maid-of-honour with
her, and for the last two months off and on she
hasn’t been expected to live. Sometimes she gets
a little better, and then Miss Vacarescu isn’t
expected to live, or else they both recover fora
tune so that the broken-hearted Prince Ferdinand
can take a turn at Death’s door. The King bore
up against this domestic affliction as long as pos-
sible, but'last week he was making up his mind
(so the cable stated) to abdicate the Throne, and
if he goes the country will have to pawn the Crown
and shift for itself. All things considered, the
maxd-of-honour seems to deserve well of her
country.

One of the few sound Sydney Building
Societies has done a big stroke of business by
cashing in advance its own fixed-deposit receipts
minus interest and also minus a discount of eight
per cent—thus paying only £92 out of £lO9 and
interest.
One hundred and one buggies and sixty
horsemen followed the funeral of Richard Cox, of
Marrar Station, Wagga Wagga. Cox came of an
athletic, devil-may-care family. The writer has
seen one of the brothers dismount to jump a three-
railed fence, whip in hand and a heavy ulster
streaming at his heels.
It is rumoured that John Horsfall has
joined or is about to join the N.Z. Loan and
Mercantile Agency as Australian adviser. This
if true, means the taking of a very large and
influential connection to the company; it also
means big profits, provided Mr. * Horsfall can
make as good investments for the N.Z. L.M. as
he did officially for Goldsborough & Co. and for
himself in his private capacity. It will be
remembered that the celebrated Rowan pamphlet
spoke of him as the “Riverina Plunger,” the idea
conveyed thereby to outsiders being that ,T.
H. was a dangerous and reckless speculator.
It will interest many people to know that every-
one of the speculations alluded to has proved a
marvellous success.
According to Meston, Joe Fleming, who
lately died at Ipswich, Q., was one of the prin-
cipals in the capture, in N.S.W., in ’33, of the
gang of bushrangers known as “ Dick’s Mob,” or
“ Dick the Devil’s Gang,’’ consisting of seven
whites and a black gin. The gang had played out
New England and crossed over to the Namoi
country, taking refuge in a hut-on Fleming’s cattle
station. Fleming, with one Freill and a stock-
man, started for the hut, round which the bush-
rangers had dug a deep trench. Captain Dick
had his gun pointed out between the slabs, and
1 reill drew a bead on it, sending “his bullet right
down the barrel ” —Meston’s own words—“ knock-
ing Master Dick down.” Dick surrendered in
sheer funk at such tall shooting and the whole
gang was captured,
Mr. Sheldon, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), is anxious
to have marked with pegs all Crown lands open to
selection. Which recalls a story current out West
of an old-time N.S.W. Irish land agent, now
dead. On his sans the best lands were selected,
occupants ever appeared. A visit to the
offices%ot£nspect always fetched the same yam :
“ That’s Father ’s lot; this is Mr. O-—’s lot •
that s Mick L s lotand so on. But a change
came. A new officer was sent up when the Lands
Office duties were separated from those of the
C.P.S. There was quite a rush to visit the new
officer, who was rather surprised to find men
selecting where already there were selectors, but,
turning to the books, on a wrinkle given him by a
would-be selector, he found they were all blanks.
On asking his predecessor for an exolanation, th«s
old man gave an original one ; “ I kept them for
good people, who might some day want them,”

Now light and airy footsteps gliding,
A vision bright and soft providing,
Which fills me with .esthetic gladness
As in wonder nuite
I gaze, confiding
In spirits biding
Near us, so astute.
They talk about a woman's sphere
As though it had a limit !
There’s not a place in earth or heaven ;
There’s not a task to mankind given ;
There’s not a blessing, or a woe ;
There’s not a whisper “ Yes ” or “ No ”;
There’s not a life or death or birth,
That lias a feather weigjjt of worth,
Without a woman in it.
They talk about a woman’s sphere, 4
As if a man were nothing here,
And women always win it ;
There's not a nook in grim Gehenna
That hasn’t got itadeep duenna;
There’s not a whisper “ Yes,” I trow,
That can be safely sworn a “No ” ;
There’s not a mischief haps from birth
Until man’s earth returns to earth—
Without a woman in it.

THE TRUTH.
They have merit.
The fact is indisputable.
The proof lies in the free samples.
It is not enough to say they sometimes fail.
They are of human produce, and therefore fallible*
The real facts are —
Do they cure diseases of the blood and skin in the majority of in-
stances? Undoubtedly they do. ...
Do they undo the effect of a chill or congestion, by bringing about
normal conditions of health ? Yes, they loosen and disperse the congestion.
Do they permeate the blood aseptically, so that bacilli and gcims
cannot multiply and the growth of disease is checked ? Yes, they do.
Do they confer relief, assuage pain, and benefit the general health,
even when they do not cure 1 Invariably, yes.
Do they act as a natural healthy laxative, rendering them especially
valuable to women and children, and to men of sedentary habits of life or
business ? Yes, by tonic action only. ...» .
Do they free the inner skin pores and intricacies from taint, clearing
off eruptions and improving the complexion 1 They do.
Does their palatable taste and safety render them pre-eminently a
family medicine? Yes, for neither the mind nor stomach rebels against
them, and children like them.
Are they the best of all blood purifiers and the most valuable ot all
spring cleansing medicines ? Yes, they are.

The sales of Frazer’s Sulphur Tablets
afford the most convincing proof of their
merits, taken in conjunction with the methods
by which the sale has been built up.
For nearly two years past every person
who has written or applied for free samples of
Frazer’s Sulphur Tablets has been supplied
gratis and post free. Many tens of thousands
of persons have so applied, in accordance with
our invitation.
Practically every one of our advertisements
has contained an offer to supply free samples.
To the samples, therefore, as a test of
merit, must be attributed the success or failure
of the sales.
Have the sales grown?
Let the facts speak for themselves—
The sale for the year ending January 9,
1890, was 81,760 packets, proved by the pur-
chase of that number of Revenue Stamps.
The sale for the year ending January 9,
1891, was 412,000 packets, proved also by the
purchase of that number of Revenue Stamps,

The Shame bf Going Bach..
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When you’ve borne to inaka a fortune and yoft
haven’t made your salt,
And the reason of your falluro isn’t anybody’s
fault—•
When you haven’t got a billet, and the times are
Very slack,
There is nothing that can spur you like the shame
of going back;
Crawling home with empty pockets,
Going back hard-up,
Oh ! it’s then you learn the meaning of “ humilia-
tion’s cup.”
When the place and you are strangers and you
struggle all alone,
And you have a mighty longing for the town where
you are known;
When your clothes are very shabby and the
future’s very black,
There is nothing that can hurt you like the shame
of going back ;
Going home with empty pockets,
Crawling back hard-up;
Oh ! it's then you know the meaning of “ humilia-
tion’s cup.”
When we’ve fought the battle bravely and are
beaten to the wall,
’Tis the sneers of men, not conscience, that make
cowards of us all;
And the while you are returning, oh, your brain is
on the rack,
And your heart is in the shadow of the shame of
going back;
Going home with empty pockets,
Going back hard-up;
Then you know the bitter meaning of “ humilia-
ton’s cup.”
When a beaten man’s discovered with a bullet in
his brain
They post-mortem him, and try him, and they say
he was insane ;
But it very often happens that he’d lately “got
the sack,”
And his onward move was owing to the shame
of going back;
Going back with empty pockets,
Going home hard-up;
Oh ! it’s then you know the meaning of “humilia-
tion’s cup.”
Ah! my friend, you call it nonsense, and your
sneering lip is curl’d,
I can see that you have never worked your passage
thro’ the world ;
But when fortune rounds upon you and the rain is
on the track,
You will learn the bitter meaning of the shame of
going back;
Crawling home with empty packets,
Going home hard-up;
Oh, you’ll taste the bitter poison in “ humiliation’s
cup. ”
Sydney, Sept., ’9l.
HENRY LAWSON.
A supposed member of the Kolao Hui
secret society was arrested recently at Wusuch.
The Huangchow Commandant sliced off Ins ears’
and, after two days’ spell, sent him on to Wuchang
“with a string through his collar-bone.”
A Socialism under ruling commercial conditions
—the Co-operative Irrigation Colonies.
An old fishmonger, of Parnell, near
Auckland, N.Z., the other day suicided in sheer
despair at his inability to earn a living. He left
behind him a wailing screed stating that he had
made “ just Is. 6d. in eight weeks.” Ghost of all
misery!
The Co-Operative Irrigation Colonies.
The Parnellites, it is cabled, purpose boy-
cotting the Dublin Freeman’s Journal. When
Daniel O’Connell boycotted Brewer Guinness,
he laid the foundation of the Guinness family’s
colossal fortune. The beer-man had to export his
liquor, for which honest beverage he found markets
all over the world. However, there would be no
export market for the Freeman.

When near B ngera the bag looked rather “flabby ”

SHE PUT IT IN THE CUPBOARD.
What a weary thing it is to be ill, especially for a
long time. You are tired of advice, tired of try-
mg this, that, and the other, tired of fancying you
are a trifle better, tired of taking precautions
against getting worse. You are tired of pain, and
half believe the sooner you are under the ground
the better for you, and the more agreeable to your
friends.
Here’s a little story. A woman told it lately.
She says;
[Copy. ]
“I, Mary Jones, of 3, Gal ton-street, Great
Howard-street, Liverpool, declare as follows i
“I have suffered all my life from Weakness of
the stomach and sluggish liver. I was always
tired and languid, and often troubled with bilious
headache. -My "Ppetite was poor. I never li&d
desire for food, and what I did eat did not digest
properly. I had always a bad taste in my mouth,
and a slimy scum would cover my mouth and
teeth, and so bad was this that I was obliged to
rinse it away before eating. When I was in my
teens I was very weakly and would faint after
sitting down to my meals. I bad trembling at
the healt, with a heavy weight at the chest, pain
at my side, and a strange giddy feeling would
come over me. Winn out walking in the street I
used to have to stand and rest for fear of falling,
I was also so Weak that when going about my
work I often bad to sit d wn and rest. At times
I had a bad cough and pain in my lungs. I saw
doctor after doctor, and was under medical treat-
ment for more than twelve 3’ears. One doctor
said I was suffering from an enlargement on the
liver, another said I had heart disease, a third
said my lungs were congested and that I could not
recover. 1 hinking I was now in a consumption,
I went to a consumptive hospital and was under
treatment there for some time. The doctors, after
sounding my chest, told me I was in a decline,
and that my left lung vas congested. They gave
me rod bur oil and other medicines, but I got no
better.
“In July, 18C0, after a bad fainting, I became
so bad I bad to take to mj r bed, and was attended
by three, doctors for a month. The first doctor
who saw me said I could not recover. I had
intense pains in my stomach, and could get
nothing to i ass my bowels. Various means were
tried and found useless. I could take liquid food
only, and vas fast sinking, my case being con-
sidered hopeless by both the physician and the
nurse. At tins time it was that my husband
heard through Mr. Parry, the chemist in Great
Howard-street, of a medicine called Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and wo were presented
with a book which describe d a case just like mine
having been cured by it. My husband procured
a bottle of this medicine and I began taking it.
After a few doses I passed a motion ns black as
coal and experienced great relief, and shortly after
felt as if I wanted something to eat. When the
doctor called the next day he could see a marked
change in me and said, ‘Mrs. Jones, wo have
given you the right medicine at last.’ He thought
it was his last bottle that bad worked the change,
whereas the nurse had put the medicine in the
cupboard untouched. 1 went on with Mother
Sei gel's Syrup, and to the delight of my husband,
father, and mmo I never looked behind me All
the pain at the heart and chest gradually left me,
and in a fortnight I was strong enough to bo
removed to the seaside, and have ever since been
in good health.
“I never felt so well in my life as I do now.
All my friends consider the cure as a miraculous
one, and I wish others to know by what means
my life was saved. lam willing that Messrs. A.
.1. White, Limited, shall make whatever use they
Tn „,- ci ftt of this statement, Q nd I hereby
authorise them to do so.
‘Y.i.id tins 3rd April, 1891.
“ (Signed) Mrs. Mary Jones.”
There was nothing “miraculous” about this
lady's recovery, although it may have been, and
indeed it was, remarkable. But it was all along
the straight lines of nature. She had suffered all
her life from indigestion and dyspepsia, and her
other ailments arose from that, and that alone.
She could not digest her food, and the whole sys-
tem was perishing from tho poison in the stomach
and from lack of nutrition. Mother Siegel's
Syrup did its one work : it cleansed away the cor-
rupted matter and set the digestive organs (the
liver, stomach, and bowels) in natural operation,
and immediate improvement and final recovery
followed as necessary consequences.
Especially should the reader notice the close
resemblance between the symptoms of dyspepsia
and those of consumption, through which most
unhappy mistakes are constantly made. Never
conclude you have consumption until you are sure
it isn’t dyspepsia. The chances are that the
results will be the same as in Mrs. Jones’ case.

To 11. F. Kugelmann - , Esq.
Dear Sir, —IV,is is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. / consulted
moie than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine witho it it doing me the sli htest
good, but 1 bee unc worse every day, wasting away until
f oecain3 a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up Mood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectoratingevery day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
You told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
*Ed of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
rung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
uesnonal sk'" I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before mo, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
Bounty of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1838.

In answer to ex-Sergoant-Major Giles, of
Maitland Point, Sofala (N.S.W.), we have to say
that the oldest N.S.W. police-pensioners are—
Edward Wilson, trooper, Penrith Police, who,
since July 1, 1850, has drawn £l4 18s. 3d. per
annum. From the Police Fund James Smith,
ordinary constable, Sydney, has enjoyed 3s. per
day since July, 1857 ; and Michael Cassidy, ser-
geant of . the Western Gold Police, has had Is. (Id.
per day since Feb. 1, 1859. Sergeant Giles dates
his pension from July 1, 1859. An old pensioner
still appears on the list in the person of F. L. S.
Merewether, who lias drawn £909 per annum
since Jline, 1850.

Art and Vice-Royalty.
Neither Governor nor Lady Jersey bought any
pictures at the recent N.S. W. Art Society’s show.
We are rather surprised at this. You might have
given the poor artists a turn, Jersey. It wouldn't
have co t much—say a fiver or so. You could
have got a nice little picture for a fiver—and what
is a fiver to you? You must have saved that
amount by walking to Randwick races and going
in on the nod, instead of paying cab-fares and
gate-money. Or Lady Jersey ought to have made
a fiver out of the S.M. Herald or Telegraph for
those vei’ses about “Who were all these people?”
And think what a lot it would have meant to the
artists. Immediately it was known that the Earl
or Countess had bought a picture, all Potts Point
and Double Bay would have galloped their
carriage- horses down to the rooms, and bought the
loudest-coloured pictures they could see. Even
if the aides-de-camp had bought one or two it
would have given the show a great lift. This is a
land of grovel, cf boot licking grovel in high
places. And the retired tallow-merchants and
whisky-dealers, who constitute the aristocracy,
are ready enough to encourage art or anything
else with their cheque-books, if they get a notion
that it will find them favour in the eyes of
Government House. So long as they can talk to
their acquaintances about the “picture I bought
at the Art Society—it’s ’anging right alongside o’
the one Jersey bought,” then they are happy. It
would have made a vast difference to the artists,
and only a fiver to you, Gov. We wonder you
didn’t do it.
* * *
From the downy and artful Carington we
wouldn't have expected it. It was more within
his province to carry the crumpled-up remains of
a footballer home in his carriage, or to weep over
aheap of damaged bedding after a d rap r’s fire.
And from his aides-de-camp we would certainly
not have hoped anything. Liquor was their
speciality. So long as anyone would find the
liquor they would always provide the thirst.
Brains they didn’t have—only throats. But these
present aides are reputed to be rather a cultivated
lot of young men, with souls to some extent above
bottles. And yet even they have turned coldly
away from the efforts of the struggling children of
art. And from Lady Jersey, the refined, poetical,
kind-hearted Lady Jersey, the painter men hoped
so much. Toiling away in their garrets, living
mostly on credit and tinned fish, they slaved at their
pictures, hoping against hope. They knew that
unless some one showed the way the Sydney
plutocrats would never dream of buying pictures.
They knew that the same men who would refuse
to sit behind a carriage-horse wo;th less than a
hundred guineas, would complacently decorate the
walls of their houses with gaudy oleographs
supplied in bulk by the furniture-dealers.
They knew that in submitting their pictures to
these people, they were literally casting pearls
before swine. But with the Jersey Regime all
this was to change, and a taste for art was to
arise. So they opened their show with high
hopes, and Jersey came and ambled round the
room and said “Nice picture, ain’t it?” before
each one. And Lady Jersey admired and went
away : and Darling Point climbed heavily into its
carriages and rumbled away behind its hundred-
guinea horses, feeling quite sure that as the
Go’vment ’Ouse party hadn’t bought any pictures
it would be a risky and bizarre thing for them to
try. And the artist men were left lamenting.
Certain captious critics in the Victorian
Assembly are finding fault with the Anglican
Church for having leased (long ago) the Collins*
street frontage of St. James’ (Melb.) to a brewery.
One member—a humourless person with no sort of
turn for finance —said “he had heard ministers
preach against drinking whisky as a most dis-
graceful thing. At the same time it appeared in
this case that the Church of England let a portion
of land for brewery purposes. The land under
review had a grand brewery frontage to Collins-
street, but people had to go around into Little
Collins-street to discover the church.” Another
member insinuated that two churches, a back and
front tabernacle, ought to be there, because the
setting apart of so much of the original site for
the worship of Bacchus left many people in that
district “unprovided for. ’ Unprovided for, when
the larger institution turns out thousands of
barrels every year ! The Bulletin doesn’t often
whoop for an Established Church, but in this
instance we feel inclined to shout hallelujah as
loud as the “ General ” himself. It is the con-
junction of Beer with the Bible that has made
England great, and where the Church goes in for
bottling by deputy, as it were, any Australian
Parliament Is justified in singing “ God Save the
Queen.” Meanwhile we will sing :

“ I never had such a jolly time in my life
after the first shock was over,” said a genial-
looking edition of Dundreary grown milclewed,
a little while ago, reciting his experiences in Dar-
linghurst Prison, after escaping a ten years’sen-
tenee by a three years’ servitude. “Why,”
continued he, “I met the banker who used to
allow me my overdrafts, and the broker with
whom I had had the last razzle-dazzle, and then I
saw coining in that old ourang-outang who
used to be the palladium of our liberty. Why! I
was in the company of the best ‘ toffs,’ and we
soon assimilated, and we made a Mafia for our-
selves, protect! ve and aggressive if need be ! An
old codger, who said he used to be private secre-
tary to a Minister in another colony, was my
washerman for a quarter of tobacco weekly, and
a clergyman used to supply me with laughter and
satire upon ‘revealed ’ religion.”
On one day in March, 1822, at Hobart
Town, Yandemonia, the Rev. Dr. Knopwood
sentenced seven men to (550 lashes .among them,
in addition to a mere trifle of other punishments
aggregating l7f years at Macquarie Harbour, On
one March day in 1891, at Liverpool, England,
Mr. Justice Day ordered 10 criminals to receive
among them 12 floggings of 20 lashes each—in
addition to sentences totalling 29 years’ imprison-
ment. Problem : Find, from the equation of the
ten Jrc j* which British justice has been
modified by the diffusion of humanitarian ideas.

are left open in Ballarat, a city of 30,000 in-
habitants.
Labour Disputes and the Way out—the Co-
operative Irrigation Colonies.
At a N.S.W. Circuit Court the only orator
of the Australian bar was present while a junior
was defending. During the case, the orator,
who says that he is a great linguist, handed
the junior a paper on which was written, “ Bcgo
bstha tshurd ” or words to that effect, and ex-
plained, “That is Dutch for ‘y° u are doing most
excellently.’” The junior was ungratefully scorn
ful, but said nothing. After the address for the
defence the senior handed up another paper
inscribed, “ Caraballo spraken gibberish,” or some-
thing that looked like that, and said, “That is
Peruvian for ‘you have made.a wonderful speech. ’”
The junior scribbled the name of the orator on
another scrap of paper and handed it to him with
the remark, “That is English for blankety blank
old fool.” And since then they never have been
such friends as of yore.
W. Hutchison, M.H.R., of Maoriland, is
going to move in the Wellington Parliament that
his country should start afresh with a new name,
and forget the dismal title which it borrowed from
the frog-infested swalnps of Zeeland. Hutchison
is just a little too subsequent. The Bulletin has
already changed the name of his country, to
Maoriland (M.L. for short), and he is arguing
about an accomplished fact, and valiantly whack-
ing a corpse. Moreover, from the letter which
Hutchison writes to the Evening Post, he
evidently thinks that the original Zeeland was
part of Denmark, and he wails in an inconsequent
way about the Baltic Sea* Such ignorance is dis
gusting.

BEER AND SPIRIT.
Man wants but little here below,
He wants but little here
Of many other things, although
He wants a lot of beer.
And when ’tis brewed on holy ground
From simple hops and malt
(The mixture being tickled round
With little sticks of salt)
For better tipple far and wide
A man would have to searcli ;
That frothy brew seems sanctified
And issued by the Church.
As well for body as for soul
The Church must have a care,
And liquor strengthens, on the whole,
A sinner more than prayer.
The shepherd likes denouncing drink,
We’ve always understood,
But still admits, with pious wink,
A drop or two is good,
No bigot railers shall prevent
The servants of the Lord
From letting brewers pay the rent
Which bishops can’t afford.
When bishops hold two plots of sod
They dedicate, no fear,
The cheaper to Almighty God,
The better one to beer.
So, if a brewery you find
When Collins-street you search, *
Why, raise your hat, for close behind
There stands a shabby church.
Betwixt those buildings you may choose
Your flagging heart to cheer :
For one the Holy Spirit brews,
The other —Holy Beer.
